The Fourth Figure: Definition, Limitations, and Moods
Definition and limitations of the fourth syllogistic figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a categorical syllogism and determine why it cannot yield a universal affirmative conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure inverts the first, but its structure prevents universal affirmative conclusions.

The fourth figure is the mirror image of the first, with the middle term subject in the minor and predicate in the major. While it can produce valid conclusions, it has a notable limitation: it never yields a universal affirmative. In this lesson, we will dissect the author's reasoning and understand the conditions that govern valid moods in this figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term structure of the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. This is opposite to the first figure. Read the author's definition and trace the term positions in your mind.
The author says: الأوسط فيه موضوعا في الصغرى محمولا في الكبرى
2
Analyze why universal affirmative is impossible
The author argues that the minor term may be more general than the middle, and the major more specific. This prevents a universal affirmative conclusion. Consider an example where the minor term is broader than the middle.
If the minor term is more general, then 'all S are P' cannot hold because P (major) may be more specific than S.
3
Examine the reduction failures
The author lists why reduction to other figures fails: first figure would require a converse which is not universal affirmative; second gives only negative; third gives only particular. This shows the limitation is inherent.
Reduction (رد) is a method to test validity by converting to the first figure.
4
List the conditions for valid moods
The author states that of the 16 possible combinations from 4×4, some are dropped due to conditions, e.g., the particular negative (O) cannot be used because it would require the universal affirmative (A) as the other premise, and two particulars yield nothing.
This is a general syllogistic rule: no conclusion from two particulars.
5
Apply the conditions to a real example
Test a fourth-figure syllogism from Islamic sciences. For instance, in Fiqh: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor, A), 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major, A). The middle term 'intoxicant' is predicate in minor and subject in major (fourth figure). The conclusion 'Nabidh is forbidden' is particular, not universal.
This demonstrates that the fourth figure cannot yield a universal affirmative like 'All nabidh is forbidden'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [في الشكل الرابع]
أما الشكل الرابع وهو الذي يكون الأوسط فيه موضوعا في الصغرى محمولا في الكبرى على عكس الأول فذلك راجع إلى الاستدلال باجتماع الأمرين في البعض من الثالث. ولذلك لا ينتج موجبة كلية فإنه يحتمل أن يكون الأصغر الذي حمل على كل الأوسط أعم، والأكبر الذي على كلية الأوسط أخص. وأما قول من قال إنه لا ينتج موجبة كلية لما أنه لا يمكن أن يتبين بالرد إلى الشكل الأول فإنه إذا أمكن كان عكس النتيجة من اللوازم؛ والموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس موجبة كلية، ولا بالرد إلى الثاني أيضًا؛ وذلك لا ينتج إلا سالبة، ولا بالرد إلى الثالث كذلك؛ وذلك لا ينتج إلا جزئية. فذلك لا يصح إلا وأن يكون البيان منحصرًا في الرد إلى الأول أو إلى غيره؛ وليس كذلك لما نبينه من بعد.
ثم الضروب الحاصلة في هذا الشكل هي ما يحصل في غيره من الأشكال. وإنها بالنسبة إلى المحصورات لا غير هي ما يحصل من ضرب الأربعة في الأربعة لكن البعض منها ساقط لما فيه من الشروط : [١] منها أن لا تكون السالبة الجزئية مستعملة فيه؛ إذ لو كانت مستعملة لكانت مستعملة مع الموجبة الكلية لا محالة لاستحالة استعمالها مع الموجبة الجزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين،
Section: [On the Fourth Figure]
As for the fourth figure, which is the one in which the middle term is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise, opposite to the first figure, it reduces to inference by the combination of two things in some part of a third. Therefore it does not produce a universal affirmative, for it is possible that the minor term, which is predicated of the whole middle, is more general, and the major term, which is predicated of the whole middle, is more specific. As for the statement of one who said that it does not produce a universal affirmative because it cannot be shown by reduction to the first figure—for if possible, the converse of the conclusion would be necessary, and the universal affirmative does not convert as a universal affirmative—nor by reduction to the second, which only produces a negative, nor by reduction to the third, which only produces a particular. That is not valid unless the demonstration is limited to reduction to the first or to something else, but it is not so, as we will explain later.
Then the moods generated in this figure are what are generated in other figures. With respect to quantified propositions only, they are what result from multiplying four by four, but some are dropped due to conditions: [1] among them is that the particular negative is not used in it; for if it were used, it would necessarily be used with the universal affirmative, because it is impossible to use it with the particular affirmative; and there is no syllogism from two particular premises.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author defines the fourth figure as one where the middle term serves as subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise, inverting the order of the first figure. This figure essentially infers from two things coinciding in part of a third.

The author then explains why the fourth figure cannot yield a universal affirmative conclusion: because it is possible that the minor term (subject of the conclusion) is more general than the middle, and the major term (predicate of the conclusion) is more specific. Reductions to other figures fail due to conversion limitations—a universal affirmative does not convert to a universal affirmative, and other figures only give negative or particular conclusions.

Next, the author discusses the moods of this figure. Since there are four types of quantified propositions (A, E, I, O), the total possible combinations are 16 (4×4). However, some are invalid due to general syllogistic conditions: in particular, the particular negative (O) cannot be used because it would require the universal affirmative (A) as the other premise, but two particular premises cannot yield a conclusion. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the context indicates further conditions will follow.

For example, consider: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (A), 'Some drink is an intoxicant' (I). This yields 'Some drink is forbidden' (I). But if we try to get a universal affirmative, say 'All drinks that are intoxicants are forbidden', the middle term arrangement in the fourth figure may not support it.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure cannot yield a universal affirmative conclusion, a crucial constraint when evaluating syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Fourth Figure: Definition and Limitation
Definition and limitation of the fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism by its middle term arrangement and explain why it cannot produce a universal affirmative conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's structure prevents universal affirmative conclusions due to term generality relations.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism as defined by the author. We will learn its unique property: despite being reducible to the third figure, it cannot yield a universal affirmative conclusion. We will also consider an objection about reduction to other figures and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Define the fourth figure
Identify that in the fourth figure, the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise, opposite to the first figure.
2
Understand the limitation
Note the author's argument: the fourth figure cannot produce a universal affirmative because the minor term (predicated of all of the middle) may be more general than the major term (predicated of all of the middle), making a universal affirmative conclusion invalid.
3
Examine the objection
Consider the claim that the failure is due to impossibility of reduction to the first, second, or third figures to produce a universal affirmative.
4
Evaluate the refutation
Recognize that the author rejects this objection by stating that reduction is possible (to be shown later), so the objection's premise is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [في الشكل الرابع] أما الشكل الرابع وهو الذي يكون الأوسط فيه موضوعا في الصغرى محمولا في الكبرى على عكس الأول فذلك راجع إلى الاستدلال باجتماع الأمرين في البعض من الثالث. ولذلك لا ينتج موجبة كلية فإنه يحتمل أن يكون الأصغر الذي حمل على كل الأوسط أعم، والأكبر الذي على كلية الأوسط أخص. وأما قول من قال إنه لا ينتج موجبة كلية لما أنه لا يمكن أن يتبين بالرد إلى الشكل الأول فإنه إذا أمكن كان عكس النتيجة من اللوازم؛ والموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس موجبة كلية، ولا بالرد إلى الثاني أيضًا؛ وذلك لا ينتج إلا سالبة، ولا بالرد إلى الثالث كذلك؛ وذلك لا ينتج إلا جزئية. فذلك لا يصح إلا وأن يكون البيان منحصرًا في الرد إلى الأول أو إلى غيره؛ وليس كذلك لما نبينه من بعد.
Section: On the Fourth Figure. As for the fourth figure, in which the middle term is a subject in the minor premise and a predicate in the major premise, contrary to the first, that is reducible to inference by the conjunction of two things in a part of the third. Therefore it does not produce a universal affirmative; because it is possible that the minor term, predicated of everything that is the middle, is more general, and the major term, predicated of everything that is the middle, is more specific. As for the statement of one who said that it does not produce a universal affirmative because it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first figure—for if it were possible, then the conversion of the conclusion would be entailed, and the universal affirmative does not convert as a universal affirmative—nor by reduction to the second either, for that produces only a negative; nor by reduction to the third similarly, for that produces only a particular. That is not valid unless the demonstration is restricted to reduction to the first or to another; but it is not so, as we will clarify later.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism and explains a key property: it cannot produce a universal affirmative conclusion. The author begins by stating the definition: in the fourth figure, the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise—the opposite of the first figure. This is traced back to inference by the conjunction of two terms in a part of the third figure (i.e., it derives from the third figure). The reason it does not yield a universal affirmative is that the minor term (predicated of all of the middle) might be more general (a 'cins' or genus), while the major term (predicated of all of the middle) might be more specific (a 'fasl' or species). For example, if the middle is 'animal', the minor 'living being' is more general, and the major 'human' is more specific; the universal affirmative 'Every human is a living being' is actually a first-figure conclusion, not a fourth-figure one. The author then addresses an objection: someone might say that the fourth figure cannot produce a universal affirmative because it cannot be reduced to the first figure (arguing that if reduction were possible, the conclusion's conversion would be required, but a universal affirmative does not convert as a universal affirmative; and reduction to the second figure only gives a negative, to the third only a particular). The author refutes this by noting that such a statement assumes demonstration is restricted to reduction to the first or other figures, which is not the case—he will later show that reduction is possible in another way (e.g., by way of contradiction or other transformations). The key takeaway is that the fourth figure's structure inherently limits its conclusions to particular or negative propositions, not universal affirmatives.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع
الأوسط
الرد إلى الشكل الأول

C) Because the first figure cannot be converted to the fourth.
D) Because universal affirmatives require the middle term to be predicate in the minor.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure of the syllogism cannot yield a universal affirmative conclusion due to the potential generality difference between the minor and major terms when predicated of the middle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why the Negative Particular is Forbidden in the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates the valid moods of the fourth figure and explains why two types of premises are forbidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate why a negative particular premise is invalid in the fourth figure by analyzing reduction failures and constructing counterexamples.

Core Idea
The fourth figure rejects negative particulars because they break reduction to all other figures.

The author explains that of the sixteen possible premise combinations in the fourth figure, many are dropped. One key condition is that the negative particular proposition cannot be used in any premise. In this lesson, we will walk through the reasoning: why a negative particular, whether as minor or major, leads to invalidity and cannot be reduced to the first, second, or third figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases for negative particular
The passage examines two possibilities: the negative particular as the minor premise, and as the major premise. In each case, it is paired with a universal affirmative.
2
Test reduction to the first figure
If the negative particular is minor, it would need to become the major in the first figure, but a negative particular cannot be a major there. If it is major, it cannot be a minor in the first figure.
3
Test reduction to the second figure
Reduction to the second requires converting the minor premise. A negative particular does not convert (its converse is not equivalent), so reduction fails.
4
Test reduction to the third figure
Reduction to the third requires converting the major. The universal affirmative major converts to a particular, yielding two particular premises, which is invalid.
5
Conclude the prohibition
Since reduction to all three figures fails, a negative particular premise cannot yield a valid syllogism in the fourth figure. Thus, it is excluded entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الضروب الحاصلة في هذا الشكل هي ما يحصل في غيره من الأشكال. وإنها بالنسبة إلى المحصورات لا غير هي ما يحصل من ضرب الأربعة في الأربعة لكن البعض منها ساقط لما فيه من الشروط: [١] منها أن لا تكون السالبة الجزئية مستعملة فيه؛ إذ لو كانت مستعملة لكانت مستعملة مع الموجبة الكلية لا محالة لاستحالة استعمالها مع الموجبة الجزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع السالبة الكلية والجزئية أيضًا؛ ولا قياس عن سالبتين. ولو كانت مستعملة مع الموجبة الكلية فإما أن تكون هي الصغرى؛ وإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال أن يكون الأكبر فصلا للأصغر والأوسط مما يعمهما فيقال مثلاً بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، وكل ناطق حيوان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق، وإما أن تكون هي الكبرى وإنه لا ينتج أيضًا لاحتمال أن يكون الأوسط فصلا للأصغر والأكبر مما يعمهما فيقال كل ناطق إنسان، وليس كل حيوان ناطقا فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان. ولأنها إذا كانت هي الصغرى فلا يمكن بيانه بالرد إلى الشكل الأول لأن ذلك إنما يكون بجعل الصغرى كبرى والسالبة الجزئية لا تصلح أن تكون كبرى الأول، ولا إلى الثاني كذلك؛ إذ الرابع إنما يرتد إلى الثاني بعكس الصغرى، والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس. ولا إلى الثالث أيضًا؛ إذ الصغرى إذا كانت سالبة جزئية كانت الكبرى موجبة كلية لما مرّ. والرابع إنما يرتد إلى الثالث بعكس الكبرى وعكس الموجبة الكلية جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. وكذلك إذا كانت هي الكبرى فإنه لا يمكن بيانه بالرد إلى الأول فإن السالبة الجزئية لا تصلح أن تكون صغرى الأول، ولا إلى الثاني أيضًا؛ إذ الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة جزئية كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية؛ وإنما يرتد الثاني إلى الرابع بعكس الصغرى وعكس الموجبة الجزئية جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. ولا إلى الثالث كذلك؛ إذ الرابع إنما يرتد إلى الثالث بعكس الكبرى والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس. فإذن السالبة الجزئية لا تصلح للصغروية في هذا الشكل ولا للكبروية أصلا. [٢] ومنها أي من الشروط أن لا تكون الكبرى موجبة كلية إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية لاحتمال أن يكون الأوسط جنسًا للأصغر والأكبر كما يقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل فرس حيوان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان فرس.
Then the valid moods occurring in this figure are what occur in other figures. They are, with respect to quantified propositions only, what results from multiplying four by four, but some of them are dropped due to conditions: [1] Among them is that the negative particular must not be used in it; for if it were used, it would have to be used with the universal affirmative—since it is impossible to use it with the particular affirmative (and there is no syllogism from two particulars) nor with the universal negative and particular negative (and there is no syllogism from two negatives). And if it is used with the universal affirmative, then either it is the minor—and it does not produce a conclusion because the major may be a differentia for the minor and the middle which is common to both, as when one says 'Some animal is not human' and 'Every rational is an animal', for it does not yield 'Some human is not rational'—or it is the major—and it does not produce a conclusion either, because the middle may be a differentia for the minor and the major which is common to both, as when one says 'Every rational is human' and 'Not every animal is rational', for it does not yield 'Some human is not animal'. And because if it is the minor, it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first figure, for that would require making the minor a major, but the negative particular cannot be a major in the first figure; nor to the second, because the fourth reduces to the second by conversion of the minor, and the negative particular does not convert; nor to the third, for if the minor is negative particular, the major must be universal affirmative as previously, and the fourth reduces to the third by conversion of the major, and the conversion of a universal affirmative is particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars. Likewise, if it is the major, it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first, for the negative particular cannot be a minor in the first; nor to the second, for if the major is negative particular, the minor must be universal affirmative, and the second reduces to the fourth by conversion of the minor, and the conversion of a particular affirmative is particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars; nor to the third, for the fourth reduces to the third by conversion of the major, and the negative particular does not convert. Therefore, the negative particular is not suitable as a minor or as a major in this figure at all. [2] Among the conditions is that the major must not be universal affirmative if the minor is particular affirmative, because the middle may be a genus for the minor and the major, as when one says 'Some animal is human' and 'Every horse is animal', for it does not yield 'Some human is horse'.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage enumerates the valid moods of the fourth figure and details two crucial conditions for validity. The author begins by stating that the moods in this figure are derived from the multiplication of four proposition types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) by four, yielding sixteen combinations, but many are dropped due to special conditions. Condition [1]: The negative particular (saliba juz'iyya) cannot be used in any premise. The reasoning is extensive: if a negative particular were used, it would have to be paired with a universal affirmative (since two particulars or two negatives are invalid). The author then examines both possibilities: (a) the negative particular as the minor premise. For example, 'Some animal is not human' (minor) and 'Every rational is animal' (major) do not yield 'Some human is not rational' because the major ('animal') might be a differentia (fasl) of the minor ('human') and the middle ('rational') is common to both, causing a breach of the dictum de omni et nullo. (b) The negative particular as the major premise. Example: 'Every rational is human' (minor) and 'Not every animal is rational' (major) do not yield 'Some human is not animal' because the middle ('rational') might be a differentia of the minor ('human') and the major ('animal') is common to both. The author then shows that reduction to other figures fails: if the negative particular is minor, reduction to first is impossible because the negative particular cannot become a major in the first figure; reduction to second fails because it requires converting the minor, which a negative particular does not allow; reduction to third fails because the universal affirmative major converts to a particular, yielding two particulars. Similar failures occur when the negative particular is major. Hence, negative particulars are utterly excluded from the fourth figure. Condition [2]: The major premise must not be universal affirmative when the minor is particular affirmative. Example: 'Some animal is human' (minor, particular affirmative) and 'Every horse is animal' (major, universal affirmative) do not yield 'Some human is horse' because the middle ('animal') might be a genus (jins) for both the minor ('human') and the major ('horse'), so no necessary connection holds. The author demonstrates that these conditions eliminate many potential moods, leaving only a few valid ones (e.g., the traditional fourth-figure moods: Camenes, Dimaris, Fresison, etc., though not named here). The passage stresses rigorous proof by impossibility of reduction.

Key Terms
السالبة الجزئية
الموجبة الكلية
الموجبة الجزئية
عكس الموجبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the negative particular premise forbidden in the fourth figure?
A) Because it always leads to a false conclusion.
B) Because it cannot be paired with a universal affirmative.
C) Because it fails reduction to all other figures, causing invalidity.
D) Because the fourth figure only accepts universal premises.

Key Takeaway
The negative particular proposition is entirely excluded from the fourth figure because it cannot be reduced to any of the other figures, thus failing to produce a valid syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Construct a fourth figure syllogism with a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major using Islamic terms: 'Some sins are not major' (minor), 'Every act of shirk is a sin' (major). Attempt to reduce it to the first figure and explain why it fails.

Why a Universal Affirmative Major with a Particular Affirmative Minor is Invalid in the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates the valid moods of the fourth figure and explains why two types of premises are forbidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the invalid combination of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major in the fourth figure and explain why the conclusion does not follow.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative major paired with a particular affirmative minor in the fourth figure fails because the middle may be a genus of both extremes.

The second condition in the passage forbids the combination where the minor is a particular affirmative and the major is a universal affirmative. The author provides a counterexample: 'Some animal is human' (particular affirmative) and 'Every horse is animal' (universal affirmative) do not yield 'Some human is horse'. In this lesson, we will analyze why this combination is invalid and how the middle term being a genus prevents a necessary connection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor premise is 'Some animal is human' (particular affirmative). The major premise is 'Every horse is animal' (universal affirmative). Note the figure: the middle term 'animal' is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'animal'. In this syllogism, 'animal' is a genus for both extremes: 'human' is a species of animal, and 'horse' is a species of animal.
3
Check the expected conclusion
If the syllogism were valid, the conclusion would be 'Some human is horse'. However, this does not follow logically because the middle term being a genus does not necessitate a relationship between the two species.
4
Test reduction to other figures
Reduction to the first figure would require converting the major (which yields a particular) and swapping, but that leads to two particulars. Reduction to the second or third also fails due to conversion issues. The lack of necessary connection is clear.
5
Conclude the condition
Thus, when the minor is particular affirmative and the major universal affirmative, the middle term may be a genus for both extremes, making the combination invalid. This condition eliminates several potential moods.

ثم الضروب الحاصلة في هذا الشكل هي ما يحصل في غيره من الأشكال. وإنها بالنسبة إلى المحصورات لا غير هي ما يحصل من ضرب الأربعة في الأربعة لكن البعض منها ساقط لما فيه من الشروط: [١] منها أن لا تكون السالبة الجزئية مستعملة فيه؛ إذ لو كانت مستعملة لكانت مستعملة مع الموجبة الكلية لا محالة لاستحالة استعمالها مع الموجبة الجزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع السالبة الكلية والجزئية أيضًا؛ ولا قياس عن سالبتين. ولو كانت مستعملة مع الموجبة الكلية فإما أن تكون هي الصغرى؛ وإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال أن يكون الأكبر فصلا للأصغر والأوسط مما يعمهما فيقال مثلاً بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، وكل ناطق حيوان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق، وإما أن تكون هي الكبرى وإنه لا ينتج أيضًا لاحتمال أن يكون الأوسط فصلا للأصغر والأكبر مما يعمهما فيقال كل ناطق إنسان، وليس كل حيوان ناطقا فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان. ولأنها إذا كانت هي الصغرى فلا يمكن بيانه بالرد إلى الشكل الأول لأن ذلك إنما يكون بجعل الصغرى كبرى والسالبة الجزئية لا تصلح أن تكون كبرى الأول، ولا إلى الثاني كذلك؛ إذ الرابع إنما يرتد إلى الثاني بعكس الصغرى، والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس. ولا إلى الثالث أيضًا؛ إذ الصغرى إذا كانت سالبة جزئية كانت الكبرى موجبة كلية لما مرّ. والرابع إنما يرتد إلى الثالث بعكس الكبرى وعكس الموجبة الكلية جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. وكذلك إذا كانت هي الكبرى فإنه لا يمكن بيانه بالرد إلى الأول فإن السالبة الجزئية لا تصلح أن تكون صغرى الأول، ولا إلى الثاني أيضًا؛ إذ الكبرى إذا كانت سالبة جزئية كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية؛ وإنما يرتد الثاني إلى الرابع بعكس الصغرى وعكس الموجبة الجزئية جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. ولا إلى الثالث كذلك؛ إذ الرابع إنما يرتد إلى الثالث بعكس الكبرى والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس. فإذن السالبة الجزئية لا تصلح للصغروية في هذا الشكل ولا للكبروية أصلا. [٢] ومنها أي من الشروط أن لا تكون الكبرى موجبة كلية إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية لاحتمال أن يكون الأوسط جنسًا للأصغر والأكبر كما يقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل فرس حيوان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان فرس.
Then the valid moods occurring in this figure are what occur in other figures. They are, with respect to quantified propositions only, what results from multiplying four by four, but some of them are dropped due to conditions: [1] Among them is that the negative particular must not be used in it; for if it were used, it would have to be used with the universal affirmative—since it is impossible to use it with the particular affirmative (and there is no syllogism from two particulars) nor with the universal negative and particular negative (and there is no syllogism from two negatives). And if it is used with the universal affirmative, then either it is the minor—and it does not produce a conclusion because the major may be a differentia for the minor and the middle which is common to both, as when one says 'Some animal is not human' and 'Every rational is an animal', for it does not yield 'Some human is not rational'—or it is the major—and it does not produce a conclusion either, because the middle may be a differentia for the minor and the major which is common to both, as when one says 'Every rational is human' and 'Not every animal is rational', for it does not yield 'Some human is not animal'. And because if it is the minor, it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first figure, for that would require making the minor a major, but the negative particular cannot be a major in the first figure; nor to the second, because the fourth reduces to the second by conversion of the minor, and the negative particular does not convert; nor to the third, for if the minor is negative particular, the major must be universal affirmative as previously, and the fourth reduces to the third by conversion of the major, and the conversion of a universal affirmative is particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars. Likewise, if it is the major, it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first, for the negative particular cannot be a minor in the first; nor to the second, for if the major is negative particular, the minor must be universal affirmative, and the second reduces to the fourth by conversion of the minor, and the conversion of a particular affirmative is particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars; nor to the third, for the fourth reduces to the third by conversion of the major, and the negative particular does not convert. Therefore, the negative particular is not suitable as a minor or as a major in this figure at all. [2] Among the conditions is that the major must not be universal affirmative if the minor is particular affirmative, because the middle may be a genus for the minor and the major, as when one says 'Some animal is human' and 'Every horse is animal', for it does not yield 'Some human is horse'.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage enumerates the valid moods of the fourth figure and details two crucial conditions for validity. The author begins by stating that the moods in this figure are derived from the multiplication of four proposition types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) by four, yielding sixteen combinations, but many are dropped due to special conditions. Condition [1]: The negative particular (saliba juz'iyya) cannot be used in any premise. The reasoning is extensive: if a negative particular were used, it would have to be paired with a universal affirmative (since two particulars or two negatives are invalid). The author then examines both possibilities: (a) the negative particular as the minor premise. For example, 'Some animal is not human' (minor) and 'Every rational is animal' (major) do not yield 'Some human is not rational' because the major ('animal') might be a differentia (fasl) of the minor ('human') and the middle ('rational') is common to both, causing a breach of the dictum de omni et nullo. (b) The negative particular as the major premise. Example: 'Every rational is human' (minor) and 'Not every animal is rational' (major) do not yield 'Some human is not animal' because the middle ('rational') might be a differentia of the minor ('human') and the major ('animal') is common to both. The author then shows that reduction to other figures fails: if the negative particular is minor, reduction to first is impossible because the negative particular cannot become a major in the first figure; reduction to second fails because it requires converting the minor, which a negative particular does not allow; reduction to third fails because the universal affirmative major converts to a particular, yielding two particulars. Similar failures occur when the negative particular is major. Hence, negative particulars are utterly excluded from the fourth figure. Condition [2]: The major premise must not be universal affirmative when the minor is particular affirmative. Example: 'Some animal is human' (minor, particular affirmative) and 'Every horse is animal' (major, universal affirmative) do not yield 'Some human is horse' because the middle ('animal') might be a genus (jins) for both the minor ('human') and the major ('horse'), so no necessary connection holds. The author demonstrates that these conditions eliminate many potential moods, leaving only a few valid ones (e.g., the traditional fourth-figure moods: Camenes, Dimaris, Fresison, etc., though not named here). The passage stresses rigorous proof by impossibility of reduction.

الموجبة الكلية

عكس الموجبة الكلية

A fourth figure syllogism with a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major is invalid because the middle term can be a genus common to both extremes, lacking a necessary link.

Conditions and Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author explains invalid premise combinations and lists the five productive moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five productive moods of the fourth figure and explain why certain premise combinations, such as those involving a particular negative, are invalid.

Core Idea
Only five premise combinations in the fourth figure yield valid conclusions.

In this passage, the author systematically examines which premise combinations yield valid conclusions in the fourth figure of categorical syllogism. He eliminates invalid combinations one by one, leaving only five productive moods. Understanding this process will sharpen your ability to evaluate syllogistic arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for valid premises
The author lists several conditions: no two negatives; the particular negative is unsuitable as either premise; the major cannot be universal affirmative when the minor is particular affirmative; the major cannot be particular affirmative when the minor is universal negative; at least one premise must be affirmative and at least one universal.
2
Apply the conditions to eliminate invalid combinations
The first condition (no two negatives) removes seven of the sixteen possible premise combinations. The other conditions remove four more, leaving five productive moods. Count the eliminations as described.
3
List the five productive moods
The author states that five moods remain. He then describes the first mood: two universal affirmatives produce a particular affirmative. Later in the text (not fully provided), he would list the other four. For now, note that only these five are valid.
4
Analyze the demonstration of the first mood
The author provides four ways to prove the first mood: reduction to the first figure, reduction to the third figure, reductio ad absurdum, and a direct reasoning via predication. Work through each method to understand why the conclusion follows.
The passage cuts off, but the methods are clear.

And with the universal negative and the particular negative as well; and there is no syllogism from two negatives. And if they are used with the universal affirmative, then either it is the minor premise; and it does not produce because of the possibility that the major term is a differentia of the minor and the middle, such that they both encompass it; so it is said, for example, 'Some animal is not human' and 'Every rational is animal' — it does not produce our statement 'Some human is not rational'. Or it is the major premise, and it also does not produce because of the possibility that the middle term is a differentia of the minor and the major, such that they both encompass it; so it is said, 'Every rational is human' and 'Not every animal is rational' — it does not produce our statement 'Some human is not animal'. And because if it is the minor, it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first figure, because that is only by making the minor a major, and the particular negative is not suitable to be a major of the first; nor to the second similarly, for the fourth is only reduced to the second by conversion of the minor, and the particular negative does not convert. Nor to the third either, for if the minor is particular negative, the major is universal affirmative as previously. And the fourth is only reduced to the third by conversion of the major, and conversion of the universal affirmative yields a particular; and there is no syllogism from two particulars. Similarly, if it is the major, it cannot be demonstrated by reduction to the first, for the particular negative is not suitable to be a minor of the first; nor to the second either, for if the major is particular negative, the minor is universal affirmative; and the second is only reduced to the fourth by conversion of the minor, and conversion of the particular affirmative yields a particular; and there is no syllogism from two particulars. Nor to the third similarly, for the fourth is only reduced to the third by conversion of the major, and the particular negative does not convert. Therefore, the particular negative is not suitable as a minor in this figure nor as a major at all. [3] And among them is that the major should not be particular affirmative when the minor is universal negative, because of the possibility that the major is a genus of the minor and the middle, as it is said 'No human is a horse' and 'Some animal is human' — it does not produce our statement 'Some horse is not animal'. And if the particular affirmative were the minor, it would produce, as it is said 'Some animal is human' and 'No stone is animal' — so 'Some human is not stone'. [4] And among them is that one of its two premises should be affirmative, as in every one of the four figures, and universal as well, for there is no syllogism from two particulars nor from two negatives at all. So the first condition removes seven of the sixteen moods, and the other conditions remove four more, so the productive moods remain five: [1] The first mood from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying 'Every C is B' and 'Every A is C' so 'Some B is A'. Its demonstration is either by reduction to the first; that is by making the minor a major and the major a minor, producing a universal affirmative; then its conversion, which is the particular affirmative, is produced by this mood. Or by reduction to the third by conversion of the major, for it produces a particular affirmative; and it is not possible by reduction to the second, for that does not produce except a negative. Or by reductio ad absurdum, as in saying: If our statement 'Some B is A' were false, its contradictory, namely our statement 'No B is A' would be true always; and 'Every C is B' — then always 'No C is A' — then always 'No A is C'; yet it was 'Every A is C' — this is a contradiction. Or by the other method, as it is said: The minor indicates that the minor term applies to every middle, and the major indicates that the middle is predicated of every major, so it necessarily follows that the minor is predicated of every major, but it is possible that the predicate here is more general than the subject; so the certain is that some of the minor is major. And by this it appears that it does not produce a universal affirmative; and because it does not produce
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for valid syllogisms in the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi') and enumerates the five productive moods (al-durub al-muntija). The author begins by stating that a syllogism cannot be composed of two negatives, and then examines cases where a negative premise is combined with a universal affirmative. He shows that if the minor premise is a particular negative, it cannot produce a conclusion because the major term might be a differentia of the minor and middle terms. Similarly, if the major premise is particular negative, no conclusion follows. He explains that these invalid combinations cannot be reduced to the first, second, or third figures due to conversion limitations: a particular negative does not convert, and a universal affirmative converts to a particular, leading to two particulars which yield no conclusion. Then he lists three additional conditions: [2] the major must not be universal affirmative when the minor is particular affirmative (else the middle might be a genus of both extremes, as in 'Some animal is human' and 'Every horse is animal' does not entail 'Some human is horse'); [3] the major must not be particular affirmative when the minor is universal negative (else the major might be a genus of the minor and middle); however, if the particular affirmative is the minor, it is valid (e.g., 'Some animal is human' and 'No stone is animal' yields 'Some human is not stone'); [4] at least one premise must be affirmative and at least one universal, since two particulars or two negatives never produce a conclusion. The first condition (no two negatives) removes seven of the sixteen possible premise combinations, and the other conditions remove four more, leaving five productive moods. The author then describes the first mood: two universal affirmatives produce a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Every C is B' and 'Every A is C' -> 'Some B is A'). He provides four ways to demonstrate its validity: reduction to the first figure by swapping premises and converting the result; reduction to the third figure by converting the major premise; reductio ad absurdum (assuming the conclusion false leads to contradiction); and an explanation via predication (the minor applies to all middle, middle to all major, so minor applies to all major, but since predication might be broader, only a particular conclusion is certain). He concludes that this mood does not yield a universal affirmative, and the explanation is cut off.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid fourth figure syllogism on a fiqh topic: use the mood Camenes (AEE) where the major premise is universal affirmative, minor is universal negative, conclusion universal negative. For example: Every intoxicant is haram (major), No khamr is an intoxicant? That's invalid. Better: Every intoxicant is haram (major), No nabidh is non-intoxicant? Hmm. Instead, use: Every valid contract is binding (major), No sale of alcohol is a valid contract (minor), therefore No sale of alcohol is binding. Check if this fits Camenes: major: كل عقد صحيح لازم (A), minor: لا شيء من بيع الخمر بعقد صحيح (E), conclusion: لا شيء من بيع الخمر بلازم (E).

Explain why this violates the condition that the major must not be universal affirmative when the minor is particular affirmative. Then correct it by changing one premise to make it valid (e.g., change minor to 'Every human is animal' to get a valid first figure syllogism).

The Syllogism

Conditions for Productive Syllogisms
Author states two conditions for productive syllogisms and that five moods remain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid premise combinations in any syllogistic figure by applying the two conditions given in the passage.

Core Idea
Two conditions eliminate 11 of 16 moods, leaving only 5 productive ones.

Not every pair of premises yields a valid conclusion in any figure. The author provides two critical conditions that eliminate invalid moods. By understanding these conditions, you can quickly determine which premise combinations are productive in all four figures. We will unpack each condition with examples and see how they reduce the 16 possible moods to just 5.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and list the two conditions: (i) the major premise must not be a particular affirmative when the minor is a universal negative; (ii) at least one premise must be affirmative, and both must be universal (no syllogism from two particulars or two negatives).
2
Examine the third condition example
Analyze the invalid example: minor universal negative (No man is a horse), major particular affirmative (Some animal is man). Why does it fail? Because the major term 'animal' could be a genus of both 'horse' and 'man', making the conclusion uncertain.
This combination is invalid in all four figures.
3
Compare with the valid alternative
When the particular affirmative is the minor (Some animal is man) and the major is universal negative (No stone is an animal), the conclusion follows: Some man is not a stone. Notice the role reversal – now the particular affirmative is minor, satisfying the condition.
4
Apply the fourth condition
One premise must be affirmative, and both must be universal. Two particulars (I or O) or two negatives (E or O) never produce a conclusion. This eliminates 4 additional moods.
This condition holds for all figures.
5
Calculate the productive moods
The first condition alone removes 7 of the 16 possible mood combinations per figure. The other conditions remove 4 more, leaving 5 productive moods. These five are the valid moods across the four figures.
You do not need to memorize the five here; just understand the elimination process.

[٣] ومنها أن لا تكون الكبرى موجبة جزئية إذا كانت الصغرى سالبة كلية لاحتمال أن يكون الأكبر جنسًا للأصغر والأوسط كما يقال لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وبعض الحيوان إنسان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الفرس ليس بحيوان ولو كانت الموجبة الجزئية هي الصغرى فإنه ينتج كما يقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان فبعض الإنسان ليس بحجر. [٤] ومنها أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه موجبة كما في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة وكلية كذلك، فإنه لا قياس عن جزئيتين ولا عن سالبتين أصلا. فالشرط الأول يُسقط من الضروب الستة عشر سبعة، وغير الأول من الشروط يُسقط أربعةً أخرى، فبقيت الضروب المنتجة خمسة:
[3] And among them is that the major premise should not be a particular affirmative when the minor premise is a universal negative, because of the possibility that the major term is a genus of the minor and the middle, as when one says 'Nothing of man is a horse', and 'Some animal is man' – then it does not yield our saying 'Some horse is not an animal'. But if the particular affirmative were the minor, then it yields, as when one says 'Some animal is man', and 'Nothing of stone is an animal', then 'Some man is not a stone'. [4] And among them is that one of its two premises be affirmative, as in every one of the four figures, and also universal, because there is no syllogism from two particulars nor from two negatives at all. The first condition eliminates seven of the sixteen moods, and other conditions eliminate four more, so the productive moods remaining are five:
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage states two conditions that productive syllogisms must satisfy. The third condition (numbered here) forbids a particular affirmative major with a universal negative minor; the author gives examples using 'man', 'horse', and 'animal' to show why such combination fails. Specifically, 'Nothing of man is a horse' (universal negative) and 'Some animal is man' (particular affirmative) do not allow concluding 'Some horse is not an animal' because the major term 'animal' could be a genus of both 'horse' and 'man', making the conclusion uncertain. Conversely, when the particular affirmative is the minor, the combination works. The fourth condition requires that one premise be affirmative and that both be universal; no syllogism arises from two particulars or two negatives. The author then notes that the first condition alone eliminates 7 of the 16 possible premise pairs in a figure, and the other conditions eliminate 4 more, leaving exactly 5 productive moods. The commentary clarifies the logical rationale: the conditions prevent invalid inferences in all four figures.

كبرى
صغرى
موجبة جزئية
سالبة كلية
ضروب منتجة

First Mood of the First Figure: Why Only Particular Affirmative?
Author explains the first productive mood, its reductions, and why it cannot give universal conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first mood of the first figure yields a particular affirmative rather than a universal affirmative, and identify the valid reductions for this mood.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmatives yield only a particular — the minor may be more general than the major.

In this passage, the author refines the standard teaching on the first figure's first mood. While many logic textbooks claim that AAA in the first figure gives 'Every A is B', our author shows that only a particular conclusion is certain. We will explore the reasoning behind this and the various ways to demonstrate the mood's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and its premises
Find the two universal affirmative premises and the conclusion in the example: 'Every C is B' and 'Every A is C' leading to 'Some B is A'.
The author uses A, B, C as terms; note the order: C is middle, A is minor, B is major.
2
Understand why the conclusion is particular
The author argues that because the minor term (A) might be more general than the middle (C), the predicate (B) might be more specific, so only a particular conclusion is certain.
This is a critical refinement: universal conclusion would require that A is not more general than C.
3
Examine reduction to the first figure by swapping premises
By making the minor the major and vice versa, we get a universal affirmative conclusion, but its converse (particular) is what the mood yields.
This shows that the mood can be reduced to a standard universal, but the direct result is particular.
4
Examine reductions to other figures
Reduction to the third figure (by converting the major) yields a particular; reduction to the second fails because it only yields negatives.
The author explains why each reduction works or not.
5
Study the reductio ad absurdum
Assume the conclusion 'Some B is A' is false, then 'No B is A' is true. With the premises, this leads to 'No A is C' contradicting 'Every A is C'.
This indirect proof confirms the validity of the particular conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالضرب الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا كل (ج) «ب»، وكل «أ» «ج» فبعض (ب) «أ» . والبيان فيه إما بالرد إلى الأول؛ وذلك بجعل الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى ينتج موجبة كلية؛ فإن عكسها وهو الموجبة الجزئية ينتجه هذا الضرب. وإما بالرد إلى الثالث وذلك بعكس الكبرى فإنه ينتج موجبة جزئية؛ ولا يمكن بالرد إلى الثاني وذلك لا ينتج إلا سالبة. وإما بالخلف كما يقال لو كذب قولنا بعض ب أ لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من (ب) أ) دائمًا: وكان كل (ج) » («ب» فدائما لا شيء من (ج) أ) فدائما لا شيء من «أ» «ج» ؛ وقد كان كل « أ » « ج »، هذا خلف. وإما بالغير كما يقال إن الصغرى تدل على أن الأصغر على كل الأوسط، والكبرى تدل على أن الأوسط محمول على كل الأكبر فيلزم أن يكون الأصغر محمولاً على كل الأكبر لكن يحتمل أن يكون المحمول هنا أعم من الموضوع؛ فالقطعي هو أن يكون بعض الأصغر أكبر. وبهذا يظهر أنه لا ينتج موجبة كلية؛ ولأنه لا ينتج فإن الأصغر إذا حمل على الأوسط يجوز أن يكون أعم منه، والأكبر إذا حمل على كل الأوسط يجوز أن يكون أخص منه فيجوز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأعم من الأكبر فلا يجوز أن يقال كل الأصغر أكبر. وأما البيان فلا يمكن بالرد إلى الأول؛ والنتيجة في الأول وهي الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كلية، ولا إلى الثاني؛ والنتيجة فيه سالبة، ولا إلى الثالث؛ والنتيجة فيه جزئية.
So the first mood from two universal affirmatives yields a particular affirmative, as in our saying 'Every (C) is B', and 'Every A is C', so 'Some B is A'. The demonstration for it is either by reduction to the first [figure]: by making the minor the major and the major the minor, it yields a universal affirmative; then its converse, which is the particular affirmative, is produced by this mood. Or by reduction to the third [figure] by converting the major, then it yields a particular affirmative; and it cannot be reduced to the second [figure] because that only yields a negative. Or by reductio ad absurdum, as when one says: if our statement 'Some B is A' were false, its contradictory 'No (B) is A' would be true always; and since 'Every (C) is B', then 'No (C) is A' always, so 'No A is C' always; but we had 'Every A is C' — this is absurd. Or by the direct [argument], as when one says: the minor indicates that the minor term is predicated of every middle, and the major indicates that the middle is predicated of every major; so it follows that the minor is predicated of every major, but it is possible that the predicate here is more general than the subject; thus what is certain is that some of the minor is major. By this it becomes clear that it does not yield a universal affirmative. And because it does not yield [universal], when the minor is predicated of the middle, it may be more general than it, and when the major is predicated of every middle, it may be more specific than it; so it is possible that the minor is more general than the more general of the major, so it is not permissible to say 'Every minor is major'. As for the demonstration, it is not possible by reduction to the first [figure] because the result in the first [figure] is the universal affirmative which does not convert universally, nor to the second because its result is negative, nor to the third because its result is particular.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage explains the first productive mood of the first figure, where both premises are universal affirmatives. The author argues that such a mood yields only a particular affirmative (Some B is A), not a universal affirmative. Several demonstrations are provided. First, reduction to the first figure by swapping premises would yield a universal affirmative, but its conversion is only particular. Second, reduction to the third figure by converting the major gives a particular. Third, a reductio ad absurdum shows that denying the particular affirmative leads to a contradiction. Fourth, a direct argument: the minor is predicated of every middle, and the middle of every major, but the predicate may be more general than the subject, so only the particular is certain. The author then emphasizes that a universal conclusion is not possible because the minor could be more general than the major. He also clarifies that reductions fail: the first figure's universal conclusion does not convert universally, the second gives negative, the third gives particular. This is a critical refinement of the standard Aristotelian view that the first figure's first mood (AAA) yields a universal affirmative; here the author restricts it to a particular.

Key Terms
الضرب الأول (al-darb al-awwal): The first productive mood of the first figure: both universal affirmatives yielding a particular affirmative. [Note: In standard first figure (e.g. Isaghuji), the first mood (Barbara) yields a universal affirmative; this text refines it to particular.]
الرد (al-radd): Reduction of a syllogism to another figure or mood by converting premises or swapping them.
الخلف (al-khalf): Reductio ad absurdum: assuming the negation of the conclusion leads to a contradiction with the premises.
الغير (al-ghayr): Direct argument (by the meaning) without formal reduction, using subject-predicate relations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The first mood of the first figure (two universal affirmatives) yields a particular affirmative because the minor term may be more general than the middle term, making a universal conclusion uncertain.

Second Mood of First Figure: Universal Minor, Particular Major
Author presents the second productive mood of the first figure with example and reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second productive mood of the first figure, verify its validity, and reduce it to the first mood.

Core Idea
A universal minor and particular major yield a particular conclusion in the first figure.

The second mood of the first figure is a valid syllogistic form that combines a universal affirmative minor premise with a particular affirmative major premise. This mood is productive, meaning it yields a necessary conclusion. The passage introduces this mood, provides an example, and explains how it can be reduced to the first mood or proven by reductio ad absurdum. Understanding this mood is essential for mastering the complete set of valid first-figure syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the minor premise (حسنى) and the major premise (كبرى). The minor is universal affirmative (كل ج ب), the major is particular affirmative (بعض أ ج). Note the order: the author presents minor then major.
In standard first figure, major usually comes first, but here the order is reversed intentionally to show the mood.
2
Extract the terms
Identify the three terms: minor (أ), middle (ج), major (ب). In 'كل ج ب', middle (ج) is the subject of major premise and predicate of minor premise. In 'بعض أ ج', minor (أ) is subject, middle (ج) is predicate.
3
Check the conclusion
The conclusion is 'بعض ب أ' (some B is A). Verify that it follows: since every middle (ج) is major (ب) and some minor (أ) is middle (ج), then some minor (أ) is major (ب) – but note the conclusion reverses subject and predicate compared to the direct inference. The author states 'فبعض ب أ' (some B is A), which is the same as some A is B by conversion. Actually the direct inference gives 'some A is B', but the author writes 'some B is A' – this is equivalent via simple conversion.
The conclusion is particular affirmative, matching the type of the major premise (particular).
4
Understand the reduction
The author says this mood reduces to the first mood (the first productive mood: Barbara/Celarent). Reduction can be done by swapping premises and converting the conclusion, or by reductio ad absurdum as previously explained.
The reduction shows that this mood is not independent but derivable from the first mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] والضرب الثاني من موجبتين والكبرى جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا كل «ج» «ب»، وبعض ( أ ) ( ج ) فبعض «ب» «أ». والرد إلى الأول وبالخلف أيضًا كما مرَّ ولأن الأصغر محمول على كل الأوسط، والأوسط على بعض الأكبر فيكون الأصغر حاصلا لبعض الأكبر؛ فيكون بعض الأصغر أكبر.
[B] The second mood from two affirmatives with the major particular yields a particular affirmative, as in our saying 'Every C is B', and 'Some A is C', so 'Some B is A'. The reduction is to the first [figure] and by reductio ad absurdum as already mentioned, and because the minor is predicated of every middle, and the middle is predicated of some major, so the minor is predicated of some major; thus some of the minor is major.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines the second productive mood of the first figure. It consists of a universal affirmative minor (Every A is C) and a particular affirmative major (Some C is B), yielding the conclusion 'Some B is A'. The author notes that this mood can be reduced to the first figure's first mood (by swapping premises and converting) or proven by reductio ad absurdum as earlier. The direct argument: since the minor term (A) is predicated of every middle (C), and the middle (C) is predicated of some major (B), the minor is predicated of some major, so some of the minor is major. In terms of Islamic logic, for example: 'Every horse is an animal' (minor universal) and 'Some animal is a quadruped' (major particular) yields 'Some quadruped is a horse'? Actually careful: the example in the text uses C, B, A. Let's match: Every C is B: Every horse (C) is an animal (B). Some A is C: Some quadruped (A) is a horse (C). Conclusion: Some B is A: Some animal (B) is a quadruped (A). So it works.

Third Mood of the First Figure: Universal Negative Conclusion
Author explains the third mood of the first figure producing a universal negative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third mood of the first figure and construct a valid syllogism with a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major.

Core Idea
When the minor is universal negative and major universal affirmative, the conclusion is universal negative.

The third mood of the first figure combines a universal negative minor premise with a universal affirmative major premise to produce a universal negative conclusion. This mood is widely used in Islamic legal and theological reasoning to establish absolute negations. In this lesson, we will unpack its structure, reduction methods, and test it with examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood conditions
Ensure both premises are universal; the minor (first premise in figure 1) is negative, the major affirmative.
The minor is the premise containing the minor term (subject of conclusion).
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term C appears as subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
In the source, the minor is 'No C is B' and major is 'Every A is C'.
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is universal negative: No B is A, where B is the predicate of the minor and A is the subject of the major.
This is consistent with the Celarent mood in Latin.
4
Verify validity through reduction
The author mentions reduction to the first or second figure by converting the minor and then the conclusion, or by reductio ad absurdum.
Conversion of the universal negative minor yields a universal negative; then converting the conclusion gives the original form.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Apply the mood: minor 'No created being is eternal' (universal negative), major 'Every human is created' (universal affirmative), conclusion 'No eternal is human'.
This example is from kalam on the attributes of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] والثالث من كليتين والصغرى سالبة ينتج سالبة كلية كقولنا لا شيء من (ج) «ب»، وكل «أ» (ج) فلا شيء من «ب» «أ». والرد إلى الأول أو بالرد إلى الثاني. وذلك بعكس الصغرى ثمَّ عكس النتيجة، أو بالخلف كما مر ; ولأن الصغرى تدل على أن الأوسط غير حاصل لشيء من الأوسط، والكبرى تدل على أن الأوسط حاصل لكل الأكبر. فيجب أن تحصل المنافاة الكلية بين الأصغر والأكبر فلا شيء من الأصغر بأكبر. ثم الأصغر إذا نافى الأوسط نافى ملزومه وهو الأكبر.
[T] The third from two universals with the minor negative yields a universal negative, as in our saying 'Nothing of (C) is B', and 'Every A is (C)', so 'Nothing of B is A'. The reduction is to the first [figure] or to the second [figure]; that is by converting the minor and then converting the result, or by reductio ad absurdum as before; and because the minor indicates that the middle is not attained by any of the minor, and the major indicates that the middle is attained for every major; so complete opposition must occur between the minor and the major, thus nothing of the minor is major. Then if the minor opposes the middle, it opposes its necessary concomitant, which is the major.

The author offers two reduction methods: to the first figure by converting the minor premise (which yields a universal affirmative) and then converting the conclusion, or to the second figure by converting the minor, or by reductio ad absurdum. The direct argument: since the middle term (C) is not attained by any of the minor (A? Actually text says 'middle is not attained by any of the middle' – likely a copyist error: should be 'minor'? Perhaps it's 'الأوسط غير حاصل لشيء من الأصغر' i.e., the middle is not predicated of any of the minor. I'll note this in review. But the author's point is clear: complete opposition between minor and major follows because the middle is separated from the minor but connected to the major. For an Islamic example: 'No horse is a stone' (minor universal negative), 'Every quadruped is a horse' (major universal affirmative), so 'No stone is a quadruped'.

The Fourth Mood of the First Figure: Premises and Conclusion
Author explains the fourth mood of the first figure producing a particular negative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the first figure based on its premises and determine that it yields a particular negative conclusion.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative minor and universal negative major yield only a particular negative.

The author defines the fourth productive mood of the first figure, consisting of a universal affirmative minor (Every C is B) and a universal negative major (No A is C). The conclusion is not universal negative as one might expect, but particular negative: Not every B is A (i.e., Some B is not A). Let's analyze why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
In the Arabic passage, the minor premise is 'كل (ج) (ب)' (Every C is B). The major premise is 'لا شيء من «أ» (ج)' (No A is C).
Use the abstract variables to clearly see the structure.
2
Classify the premises by quantity and quality
The minor is universal affirmative (A); the major is universal negative (E).
3
Locate the conclusion
The author writes 'فليس كل «ب» «أ»' which means 'Not every B is A', i.e., some B is not A (O).
Note the conclusion is particular negative, not universal.
4
Map the terms to minor, major, and middle
The subject of the conclusion (B) is the minor term, the predicate (A) is the major term. The term appearing in both premises (C) is the middle term.
Minor premise: C subject, B predicate; Major premise: A subject, C predicate; so middle is C.
5
Verify validity intuitively
Since every C is B, and no A is C, any C that is B cannot be A. Therefore some B (those that are C) is not A. The conclusion follows.
This matches the author's direct proof at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ث] والرابع من كليتين والكبرى سالبة ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا كل (ج) (ب) ولا شيء من «أ» (ج) فليس كل «ب» «أ». والرد إلى الأول وذلك بعكس الصغرى والكبرى، أو إلى الثاني وذلك بعكس الصغرى، أو إلى الثالث وذلك بعكس الكبرى، أو بالخلف كما مرّ. ولأن الأصغر حاصل لكل الأوسط، والأوسط غير حاصل لشيء من الأكبر؛ فالأكبر إذن غير حاصل لشيء من الأوسط، وبعض الأصغر أوسط بالضرورة؛ فذلك البعض من الأصغر ليس بأكبر.
[Th] The fourth from two universals with the major negative yields a particular negative, as in our saying 'Every (C) is (B)', and 'Nothing of A is (C)', so 'Not every B is A'. The reduction is to the first [figure] by converting both the minor and the major, or to the second [figure] by converting the minor, or to the third [figure] by converting the major, or by reductio ad absurdum as before. And because the minor is predicated of every middle, and the middle is not predicated of any of the major; so the major is not predicated of any of the middle, and some of the minor is necessarily middle; therefore that part of the minor is not major.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth productive mood of the first figure, consisting of a universal affirmative minor (Every C is B) and a universal negative major (No A is C), yielding the particular negative conclusion (Not every B is A, i.e., some B is not A). The author lists multiple reduction methods: to the first figure by converting both premises, to the second by converting only the minor, to the third by converting only the major, or by reductio ad absurdum. The direct argument: since the minor term (C) is predicated of every middle (B? Actually careful: the text says 'الأصغر حاصل لكل الأوسط' – the minor is predicated of every middle. Let's align: In the example, the minor is 'كل (ج) (ب)': Every C (the middle? Actually in this mood, the major is 'لا شيء من «أ» (ج)': No A is C. So middle is C? Wait, we need to map: In first figure, middle is term appearing in both premises. Here minor premise: Every C is B (C subject, B predicate). Major premise: No A is C (A subject, C predicate). So middle is C. The minor term is B? Actually, the conclusion subject is B, predicate is A? The conclusion 'ليس كل «ب» «أ»' means 'Not every B is A' i.e., Some B is not A. So the minor term is B (the subject of conclusion), major term is A (predicate of conclusion). So the author says: 'الأصغر حاصل لكل الأوسط' means the minor (B) is predicated of every middle (C) because of the minor premise 'Every C is B' – indeed B is predicated of every C, but that is the predicate of C, not the subject. There might be confusion. But the logical flow is: from 'Every C is B' we know that whatever is C is B. From 'No A is C' we know that no A is C. Then some C is B (by conversion of the minor), and since no A is C, some B (those that are C) are not A. Hence particular negative. For an Islamic example: 'Every horse is an animal' (Every C is B), 'No stone is a horse' (No A is C). Conclude 'Some animal is not stone' (Some B is not A).

Reducing the Fourth Mood to Other Figures
Author lists reduction methods for the fourth mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce the fourth mood of the first figure to the first, second, or third figure by converting premises.

Core Idea
The fourth mood can be transformed into more standard syllogisms by conversion or reductio.

The author explains that the fourth mood can be reduced to the first figure by converting both premises, to the second by converting the minor, to the third by converting the major, or by reductio ad absurdum. Let's follow each method.

Guided Path
1
Recall the original fourth mood syllogism
Minor: 'Every C is B' (universal affirmative). Major: 'No A is C' (universal negative). Conclusion: 'Some B is not A' (particular negative).
2
Reduce to the first figure by converting both premises
Convert the minor: 'Every C is B' becomes 'Some B is C' (by conversion per accidens). Convert the major: 'No A is C' becomes 'No C is A' (by simple conversion). The new syllogism: 'Some B is C' and 'No C is A' yields 'Some B is not A'. This is a valid first-figure syllogism.
The author says 'by converting both the minor and the major'.
3
Reduce to the second figure by converting only the minor
Convert the minor to 'Some B is C', keep the major 'No A is C'. Now we have 'Some B is C' and 'No A is C', with middle term C as predicate in both premises. This is a second-figure syllogism yielding 'Some B is not A'.
The author mentions 'by converting the minor'.
4
Reduce to the third figure by converting only the major
Convert the major to 'No C is A', keep the minor 'Every C is B'. Now we have 'Every C is B' and 'No C is A', with middle term C as subject in both premises. This is a third-figure syllogism yielding 'Some B is not A'.
The author mentions 'by converting the major'.
5
Consider reductio ad absurdum
Assume the opposite of the conclusion (i.e., 'Every B is A'). Combine this with the major premise 'No A is C' to derive a contradiction with the minor premise (or a known truth).
The author says 'or by reductio ad absurdum as before'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ث] والرابع من كليتين والكبرى سالبة ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا كل (ج) (ب) ولا شيء من «أ» (ج) فليس كل «ب» «أ». والرد إلى الأول وذلك بعكس الصغرى والكبرى، أو إلى الثاني وذلك بعكس الصغرى، أو إلى الثالث وذلك بعكس الكبرى، أو بالخلف كما مرّ. ولأن الأصغر حاصل لكل الأوسط، والأوسط غير حاصل لشيء من الأكبر؛ فالأكبر إذن غير حاصل لشيء من الأوسط، وبعض الأصغر أوسط بالضرورة؛ فذلك البعض من الأصغر ليس بأكبر.

The reduction is to the first [figure] by converting both the minor and the major, or to the second [figure] by converting the minor, or to the third [figure] by converting the major, or by reductio ad absurdum as before. And because the minor is predicated of every middle, and the middle is not predicated of any of the major; so the major is not predicated of any of the middle, and some of the minor is necessarily middle; therefore that part of the minor is not major.

Why the First Mood of the Second Figure Yields a Universal Negative
Author explains why the first mood yields a universal negative conclusion and its proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first mood of the second figure cannot produce a universal affirmative conclusion using the generality relationships between terms.

Core Idea
The minor can be more general than the major, preventing a universal affirmative conclusion.

In this passage, the author investigates a specific mood of the second figure of categorical syllogism. He shows that if the minor is predicated of the middle and the major is predicated of all the middle, then the minor could be more general than the major. Consequently, a universal affirmative conclusion is impossible, and the conclusion must be negative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Terms
The passage mentions three terms: الأصغر (minor), الأوسط (middle), and الأكبر (major). In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Recognize that the minor and major are predicated of the middle.
The minor and major are predicates in their respective premises.
2
Analyze Generality Relationships
The minor, when predicated of the middle, can be more general than the middle. The major, when predicated of all the middle, can be more specific than the middle. This means the minor may be broader in scope than the middle, while the major may be narrower.
Generality here refers to the extension of the terms.
3
Combine the Possibilities
If the minor is more general than the middle and the major is more specific than the middle, then the minor is more general than the major. This directly contradicts a universal affirmative conclusion (e.g., 'Every minor is major'), which would require the minor to be at least as specific as the major.
Universal affirmative implies subject is at most as general as predicate.
4
Conclude the Valid Conclusion Type
Since a universal affirmative is impossible, the only remaining universal conclusion is a negative one: 'No minor is major'. This is the characteristic of the first mood of the second figure, which must have a universal negative conclusion.
The mood is later identified as Camestres or Cesare, both yielding a universal negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن الأصغر إذا حمل على الأوسط يجوز أن يكون أعم منه، والأكبر إذا حمل على كل الأوسط يجوز أن يكون أخص منه فيجوز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأعم من الأكبر فلا يجوز أن يقال كل الأصغر أكبر. وأما البيان فلا يمكن بالرد إلى الأول؛ والنتيجة في الأول وهي الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كلية، ولا إلى الثاني؛ والنتيجة فيه سالبة، ولا إلى الثالث؛ والنتيجة فيه جزئية.
For if the minor (term) is predicated of the middle (term), it is possible that it is more general than it; and if the major (term) is predicated of all the middle (term), it is possible that it is more specific than it; so it is possible that the minor is more general than that which is more general than the major; thus it cannot be said 'every minor is major'. As for the proof, it cannot be by reduction to the first (figure) because the conclusion in the first (figure) is universal affirmative which does not convert universally; nor to the second because its conclusion is negative; nor to the third because its conclusion is particular.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage explains the first mood of the second figure of categorical syllogism. The author examines the relationship between the minor term (الأصغر), the middle term (الأوسط), and the major term (الأكبر). He argues that if the minor is predicated of the middle, it may be more general than the middle; and if the major is predicated of all the middle, it may be more specific than the middle. Consequently, the minor could be more general than something that is more general than the major, making a universal affirmative conclusion ('every minor is major') impossible. The author then considers methods of proof (البيان) by reduction to other figures: reduction to the first figure is impossible because the first figure's conclusion (universal affirmative) does not convert universally; reduction to the second figure is impossible because its conclusion is negative; reduction to the third figure is impossible because its conclusion is particular. Thus, the conclusion of this mood must be universal negative.

الأصغر
الأوسط
الأكبر

Key Takeaway
The first mood of the second figure must have a universal negative conclusion because the minor can be more general than the major, invalidating a universal affirmative.

Why Reduction to Other Figures Fails for This Mood
Author explains why the first mood yields a universal negative conclusion and its proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the reasons why the first mood of the second figure cannot be reduced to the first, second, or third figure for proof, and understand the role of conversion and particularity.

Core Idea
Reduction fails because the first figure's affirmative conclusion doesn't convert universally, the second figure's conclusion is negative, and the third figure's conclusion is particular.

After establishing that the conclusion must be universal negative, the author examines possible methods of proof (al-bayan) by reducing the syllogism to other figures. He systematically rules out each figure: the first figure's universal affirmative cannot be converted to a universal negative; the second figure's conclusion is already negative (but that doesn't help); and the third figure's conclusion is particular, not universal. This shows the uniqueness of this mood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Goal of Reduction
Reduction aims to prove the validity of a syllogism by transforming it into a known valid mood, typically the first figure (Barbara, Celarent, etc.). The author checks if the first mood of the second figure can be reduced to any of the three figures.
The first figure is considered 'perfect' because its validity is self-evident.
2
Attempt Reduction to the First Figure
To reduce to the first figure, one typically converts a premise or the conclusion. The author notes that the first figure's conclusion in Barbara is universal affirmative. But our mood requires a universal negative. Attempting to convert the affirmative conclusion yields a particular affirmative (by conversion per accidens), not a universal negative. Hence, reduction fails.
Universal affirmative (A) converts to particular affirmative (I), not universal negative (E).
3
Attempt Reduction to the Second Figure
The second figure also yields negative conclusions, but reducing a second figure mood to another second figure mood is circular—it does not provide an independent proof. The author dismisses this as futile.
The aim is to prove the mood from a more fundamental figure.
4
Attempt Reduction to the Third Figure
The third figure always produces a particular conclusion (e.g., Some S is P). Our mood requires a universal conclusion. Since particular and universal are different quantifiers, reduction is impossible.
The third figure includes moods like Darapti (AAI) and Disamis (IAO).

As for the proof, it cannot be by reduction to the first (figure) because the conclusion in the first (figure) is universal affirmative which does not convert universally; nor to the second because its conclusion is negative; nor to the third because its conclusion is particular.

This passage explains the first mood of the second figure of categorical syllogism. Consequently, the minor could be more general than something that is more general than the major, making a universal affirmative conclusion ('every minor is major') impossible.
