Fourth Mood of the Second Figure (Particular Negative)
Author defines the fourth mood with a particular negative conclusion and its proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the second figure and demonstrate its validity using reduction or direct proof.

Core Idea
Two universals with a negative major yield a particular negative conclusion in the second figure.

In the second figure, the fourth mood combines a universal affirmative minor with a universal negative major to produce a particular negative conclusion. This mood is proven by reduction to the first figure or by a direct reasoning using the relationship of the terms. Mastering this mood sharpens your ability to analyze categorical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the two premises: the minor (subject of the conclusion) is universal affirmative, and the major (predicate of the conclusion) is universal negative. The conclusion is particular negative (not every B is A). Check the example: 'every J is B' (minor), 'nothing of A is J' (major), 'not every B is A' (conclusion).
2
Determine the mood and figure
The figure is determined by the position of the middle term (J). Here the middle is subject of the major and predicate of the minor, which characterizes the second figure. The mood is thus: minor universal affirmative (A), major universal negative (E), conclusion particular negative (O).
This mood is sometimes called Festino in the Latin tradition.
3
Understand the reduction methods
The author lists four ways to prove validity: (1) convert both premises and reduce to first figure, (2) convert only the minor and reduce to second figure, (3) convert only the major and reduce to third figure, or (4) use proof by contradiction (assume the opposite and derive a contradiction).
Conversion here means switching subject and predicate while preserving truth conditions (e.g., 'every J is B' converts to 'some B is J').
4
Trace the direct proof
The direct reasoning: 'the minor holds for all the middle' (every B is J) and 'the middle holds for nothing of the major' (no A is J). Therefore, the major holds for nothing of the middle (no J is A). Since some of the minor is necessarily middle (by conversion per accidens: some B is J), that part of the minor is not major (some B is not A).
This step uses the conversion of the universal affirmative to a particular affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من كليتين والكبرى سالبة ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا كل (ج) (ب) ولا شيء من «أ» (ج) فليس كل «ب» «أ» الرد إلى الأول وذلك بعكس الصغرى والكبرى، أو إلى الثاني وذلك بعكس الصغرى، أو إلى الثالث وذلك بعكس الكبرى، أو بالخلف كما مرّ. ولأن الأصغر حاصل لكل الأوسط، والأوسط غير حاصل لشيء من الأكبر؛ فالأكبر إذن غير حاصل لشيء من الأوسط، وبعض الأصغر أوسط بالضرورة؛ فذلك البعض من الأصغر ليس بأكبر.
The fourth mood: from two universals, the major being negative, produces a particular negative, as in our saying: every J is B and nothing of A is J, so not every B is A. Reduction to the first (figure) by converting the minor and the major, or to the second by converting the minor, or to the third by converting the major, or by contradiction as before. And because the minor holds for all the middle, and the middle holds for nothing of the major; so the major therefore holds for nothing of the middle, and some of the minor is middle of necessity; so that part of the minor is not major.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth mood of the second figure. It has two universal premises: the major premise is negative, the minor is affirmative. The conclusion is particular negative. The example: 'Every J is B, and nothing of A is J, therefore not every B is A' (i.e., some B is not A). The author lists multiple methods of proof: reduction to the first figure by converting both premises, to the second by converting only the minor, to the third by converting only the major, and by contradiction. He then gives a direct reasoning: since the minor holds for all the middle, and the middle holds for nothing of the major, the major holds for nothing of the middle. But some of the minor is necessarily middle (because the minor is predicated of all the middle, so the middle is a subset of the minor? Actually, the reasoning is that since every minor is middle (from the minor premise: كل أ ج), then some of the minor is middle (conversion per accidens). That part of the minor which is middle is not major (since the middle is wholly disjoint from the major). Therefore, some minor is not major (some B is not A). The author uses the term 'أصغر' for the subject of the conclusion (which is 'ب' in the example) and 'أكبر' for the predicate ('أ').

Fifth Mood of the Second Figure (Particular Negative)
Author defines the fifth mood with a particular negative conclusion and notes its proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood of the second figure and verify its conclusion from the given premises.

Core Idea
The fifth mood yields a particular negative conclusion from a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major.

This lesson examines the last mood of the second figure in categorical syllogisms. The passage defines its structure and provides an example. Understanding this mood allows you to recognize valid syllogisms where a particular affirmative and a universal negative produce a particular negative conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor premise is a particular affirmative: 'some J is B'. The major premise is a universal negative: 'nothing of A is J'. Note the order: the minor is mentioned first, then the major.
In this mood, the minor is particular, not universal.
2
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is 'not every B is A', which is a particular negative proposition (some B is not A). This follows directly from the premises.
The conclusion is also particular negative, matching the pattern of the second figure.
3
Determine the figure and mood
The middle term (J) appears as predicate in both premises, so it is the second figure. The mood, in the order presented, is: minor (I), major (E), conclusion (O) – i.e., IEO. This mood is valid through the reduction methods previously mentioned.
In some classifications, this mood corresponds to Festino (EIO) when the major premise is stated first.
4
Apply the proof methods
The author states that the conclusion follows 'due to the methods that have passed'. These likely include reduction to the first figure via conversion or per impossibile. For this mood, one can convert the major premise to 'no J is A' and then use the first figure to derive the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس من موجبة جزئية كبراها سالبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا بعض (ج) (ب)، ولا شيء من ( أ ) (ج) فليس كل ب أ لما مر من الطرق.
The fifth mood: from a particular affirmative, its major being universal negative, produces a particular negative, as in our saying: some J is B, and nothing of A is J, so not every B is A, due to the methods that have passed.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and last mood of the second figure. It consists of a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise. The conclusion is a particular negative. The example: 'Some J is B, and nothing of A is J, therefore not every B is A' (i.e., some B is not A). The author notes that the proof methods are the same as those previously mentioned for other moods (reduction, conversion, etc.). In standard notation, this mood corresponds to: بعض ب ج (particular affirmative), لا شيء من أ ج (universal negative), therefore ليس كل ب أ (particular negative).

سالبة كلية (universal negative)
سالبة جزئية (particular negative)
شكل ثاني (second figure)

Key Takeaway
The fifth mood of the second figure shows that a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major validly yield a particular negative conclusion.

Counting the Faces of Generality and Specificity in the Second Figure
Author enumerates the number of possible faces for each mood regarding generality and specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the number of possible faces (وجوه) for each mood of the second figure based on the generality and specificity relationships between terms.

Core Idea
The number of faces reveals how many ways the terms can be arranged in generality and specificity for syllogistic validity.

In the second figure, each mood can have multiple 'faces' (وجوه) depending on how the minor, middle, and major terms relate in terms of generality (أعم) and specificity (أخص). The author gives specific numbers for each mood. Understanding these faces helps us see the full range of valid arguments and apply them correctly in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the moods of the second figure
The second figure has five valid moods: Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco, and a fifth mood (sometimes called 'Baroco' but with different order?). List them and their standard forms.
Use the mood names from traditional logic.
2
Identify the three terms
For each mood, identify the minor term (الحد الأصغر), middle term (الحد الأوسط), and major term (الحد الأكبر).
3
Understand generality and specificity
Generality and specificity refer to the relationship between the extensions of the terms. One term is more general (أعم) if it includes the other; more specific (أخص) if it is included.
For example, 'animal' is more general than 'human'.
4
Count the faces for the first mood
The first mood has 4 faces. This comes from the possible combinations: the minor can be more general or more specific than the middle, and the middle can be more general or more specific than the major. (2×2=4).
Equal relationship is not considered as a separate face here.
5
Verify the other moods
The second mood has 8 faces (2×2×2 possibilities? Actually between the three terms there are 2 comparisons, but for this mood there is also an additional factor). The third has 2 faces, the fourth also 2, and the fifth 4.
The reasoning for these numbers may involve additional conditions like the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of premises.

Counting Valid and Invalid Faces per Mood
Author explains the number of faces per mood considering negative and affirmative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the number of valid and invalid premise combinations for any syllogistic mood in the second figure.

Core Idea
In every syllogistic mood, exactly half the premise type combinations yield a valid conclusion.

We already know that syllogistic moods have different valid forms depending on the quantity and quality of premises. Now the author expands our view: when we also consider negation (عدول) and affirmation (تحصيل), each mood has 16 possible 'faces' — that is, combinations of premise types. Surprisingly, exactly 8 are valid and 8 are invalid. This pattern holds for every mood in every figure, as we will explore.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four categorical proposition types
Each premise can be one of four types: A (universal affirmative), E (universal negative), I (particular affirmative), O (particular negative).
These are the standard types in Aristotelian logic.
2
Compute total combinations for two premises
Since each premise can be any of the 4 types, the total number of combinations for the minor and major premise is 4 × 4 = 16.
This is the '16 faces' the author mentions.
3
Identify valid combinations for the second figure
The author states, 'ثمانية منها ساقطة وثمانية باقية' (eight are invalid, eight are valid). This means that for each mood in the second figure, exactly half of the 16 possible premise pairs satisfy the figure's rules and produce a valid conclusion.
The specific valid combinations depend on the mood, but the 8/8 split is constant.
4
Generalize beyond negation and affirmation
The author notes that the same combinatorial logic applies to other variations, such as when premises have different numbers of terms (e.g., tripartite or bipartite). This shows the thoroughness of the logical analysis.
These additional dimensions further multiply the faces, but the principle of proportion remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما بالنسبة إلى العدول والتحصيل فالوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب ستة عشر : ثمانية منها ساقطة وثمانية باقية كما مر من قبل ; إذ الصغرى والكبرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون معدولة. وكما أنها متعددة بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل فكذلك بحسب الغير كما إذا كانت الصغرى ثلاثية والكبرى ثنائية أو ثلاثية على ما عرف من قبل في كل شكل.
As for with respect to negation and affirmation, the faces occurring in each mood are sixteen: eight of them are invalid and eight are valid, as has passed before; for the minor and major premises may be affirmative (محصلة) or they may not be, rather they may be negative (معدولة). And just as they are multiple according to generality and specificity and negation and affirmation, so too according to other (considerations), such as when the minor is tripartite and the major is bipartite or tripartite, as was known before in every figure.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage discusses another dimension of variation for the moods of the second figure: whether the premises are affirmative (محصلة) or negative (معدولة). The author states that for each mood, there are 16 possible face, with 8 being valid and 8 invalid. This is a general principle mentioned earlier. Additionally, beyond generality/specificity and negation/affirmation, there are other considerations such as the number of terms in each premise (e.g., three-term premises vs. two-term premises). These complex variations highlight the thoroughness of the logicians in analyzing all possible forms of the syllogism.

Objection to the Second Figure's Independence and Response
Author presents and refutes the objection that the second figure does not produce by itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that the second figure is not independent, and evaluate the author's refutation based on logical consequences.

Core Idea
Reduction to the first figure does not negate the second figure's independent validity.

In the study of categorical syllogisms, the second figure is sometimes criticized as not being truly independent, because its conclusion can be obtained by conversion from the first figure. This lesson examines that objection and the author's defense. Understanding this debate is crucial for appreciating the structure of syllogistic logic and its application in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find in the Arabic passage the claim that the second figure does not produce by itself but by the power of the first figure. The objectors argue that the first figure produces a conclusion whose converse is the conclusion of the second figure.
Consider: if the second figure's conclusion is just a converse, it seems derivative.
2
Understand the author's initial response
The author argues that if the objection were true, then no statement would follow from the second figure by itself. But the author mentions that there are examples (الوجوه المذكورة) that show it does produce conclusions independently.
3
Analyze the first supporting point
The first point from the author: the objectors claim the second figure does not produce by itself because it needs conversion of one of its premises. But needing conversion does not negate self-production; the figure still produces a valid argument.
Contrast this with the idea that reliance on conversion means the figure is not primary.
4
Consider the second point (though incomplete)
The passage suggests a second point from the author, but the Arabic breaks off. The point likely continues the refutation, perhaps by noting that the second figure has unique moods not reducible to the first.
For completeness, note that later commentators expand on these points.
5
Evaluate the overall force of the response
Assess whether the author successfully defends the second figure. Does the need for conversion really make it dependent? The author suggests that if it were dependent, no conclusion would follow, which is false.
Reflect: Can a syllogism be 'dependent' yet still produce a valid conclusion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه هذا الشكل من جملة ما تعرض لقدحه طائفة من المنطقيين وقالوا إنه لا ينتج بنفسه بل بقوة الشكل الأول فإن ذلك ينتج نتيجة عكسها هو النتيجة من هذا الشكل. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن لا يلزم عنه لذاته قول آخر ; وقد كان من الوجوه المذكورة ما يدل عليه وكذلك غيرها. وذلك من وجوه أيضًا : [۱] منها أن يقال إنه لا ينتج لذاته وإلا لا يكون مفتقرا في الإنتاج إلى عكس إحدى مقدمتيه بل إلى عكس كل واحدة منهما; وقد كان مفتقرًا بالضرورة. [٢] ومنها أن يقال إنه من جملة ما لم يتعرضوا
Then it is possible for a speaker to say about this figure that it is among those things that a group of logicians attacked, and they said that it does not produce by itself but by the power of the first figure, because that (the first figure) produces a conclusion whose converse is the conclusion of this figure. And if that were the case, it would be among the necessary consequences that no other statement would follow from it by itself; and there are among the mentioned faces that indicate it, and likewise others. And that is also among the faces: [1] from them is that it is said that it does not produce by itself, otherwise it would not need in producing to convert one of its premises, but rather to convert each one of them; and it was necessarily in need. [2] and from them is that it is said that it is among what they did not address...
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the second figure's independence. A group of logicians argued that the second figure does not produce conclusions by itself; rather, it relies on the first figure because the conclusion of the first figure, when converted, gives the conclusion of the second figure. The author responds by stating that if that were true, then no statement would follow from the second figure by itself, yet there are examples (الوجوه المذكورة) that show it does. He then lists two points: first, the objectors claim that the second figure does not produce by itself because it needs conversion of one of its premises (or both). But the author implies that needing conversion does not negate self-production; the figure still produces a valid argument. The second point is incomplete but likely continues the refutation. The author defends the second figure's validity as an independent form of argument, which is a standard position in Aristotelian logic.

Key Terms
بقوة الشكل الأول (bi-quwwat al-shakl al-awwal): By the power of the first figure; meaning that the validity of the second figure derives from the first figure through conversion.

The Fifth Mood of the Fourth Figure
Enumeration of the fourth figure's moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood of the fourth figure and verify its validity using an Islamic example.

Core Idea
The fourth figure has five moods; the fifth combines a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is often presented with only four moods, but our author includes a fifth. Understanding this mood requires careful attention to the quantity and quality of the premises and the arrangement of terms. This lesson will break down the fifth mood step by step and show you how it works in an Islamic theological context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Locate the minor premise in the Arabic: it is 'موجبة جزئية' (particular affirmative). In the example, 'بعض (ج) (ب)' (Some C is B). In Islamic terms, think of C as 'some acts of worship' and B as 'obligatory'.
2
Identify the major premise
The major premise is 'سالبة كلية' (universal negative): 'ولا شيء من (أ) (ج)' (No A is C). Replace A with 'that which is forbidden' and C with 'acts of worship'.
3
Determine the conclusion
The conclusion is 'سالبة جزئية' (particular negative): 'فليس كل ب أ' (Not every B is A). That means 'some B are not A'. For our example: 'some obligatory acts are not forbidden'.
4
Verify the mood's validity
Check the rules of the fourth figure: the middle term is the predicate of the minor and the subject of the major. Here the middle term is C (acts of worship). The minor premise has C as subject of B? No: 'بعض (ج) (ب)' means C is subject, B predicate. The major has A as subject, C predicate. So middle term is C, predicate in minor and subject in major – that fits the fourth figure. The mood is valid.
5
Construct an Islamic example
Now create your own: minor: 'Some prayers are obligatory' (موجبة جزئية). Major: 'No forbidden act is a prayer' (سالبة كلية). Conclusion: 'Some obligatory acts are not forbidden' (سالبة جزئية). Check that the terms are in the right order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس من موجبة جزئية كبراها سالبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا بعض (ج) (ب)، ولا شيء من ( أ ) (ج) فليس كل ب أ لما مر من الطرق. ثم كل واحد من هذه الضروب الخمسة يقع على وجوه مختلفة أيضًا بحسب اختلاف الأحوال في حدودها من العموم والخصوص وغير ذلك: أما الضرب الأول فذلك على أربعة أوجه باعتبار العمومية والخصوصية بين الأصغر والأوسط، والأوسط والأكبر. وأما الثاني فعلى ثمانية أوجه، وأما الثالث فعلى وجهين، وأما الرابع فكذلك على الخلاف خامس؛ فإنه على أربعة أوجه هذا بالنسبة إلى العموم والخصوص. فأما بالنسبة إلى العدول والتحصيل فالوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب ستة عشر : ثمانية منها ساقطة وثمانية باقية كما مر من قبل؛ إذ الصغرى والكبرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون معدولة. وكما أنها متعددة بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل فكذلك بحسب الغير كما إذا كانت الصغرى ثلاثية والكبرى ثنائية أو ثلاثية على ما عرف من قبل في كل شكل.
And the fifth (mood) is from a particular affirmative minor with its major being a universal negative, producing a particular negative, like our saying: 'Some C is B, and no A is C, so not every B is A' according to the previously mentioned methods. Then each one of these five moods falls into different aspects also according to the difference of states in their terms with respect to generality, particularity, and other things: As for the first mood, that is in four aspects considering the generality and particularity between the minor and middle, and the middle and major. As for the second, it is in eight aspects; as for the third, in two aspects; as for the fourth, likewise, on the contrary, the fifth: for it is in four aspects, this is with respect to generality and particularity. As for with respect to counter-change and affirmation, the aspects occurring in each mood are sixteen: eight of them are invalid and eight remain, as previously mentioned; for the minor and major may be affirmative, and may not be but rather be counter-changed. And just as they are multiple according to generality and particularity and counter-change and affirmation, so also according to otherness, such as when the minor is tripartite and the major is bipartite or tripartite, as has been known before in each figure.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It first gives an example of the fifth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, a particular negative conclusion follows. The example uses the placeholder variables C, B, A. Then it explains that each of the five moods has multiple aspects (وجوه) based on the relations of generality and particularity between the terms (minor, middle, major). For the first mood, there are four aspects; the second has eight; the third has two; the fourth (and similarly the fifth) has four. Next, it considers the aspect of whether the propositions are affirmative (محصلة) or counter-changed (معدولة, i.e., with a negated subject or predicate). Each mood then yields sixteen possible forms, of which eight are invalid and eight valid, because the minor and major can each be affirmative or counter-changed. Finally, it mentions further variations (الغير) such as the number of terms in the premises—e.g., whether the minor is tripartite (three terms) and the major bipartite (two terms) or tripartite—as established earlier for other figures. This systematic breakdown illustrates the complexity of the fourth figure's moods and the need to consider multiple logical dimensions.

Mood Variations by Generality and Particularity
Classification of mood variations by generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the number of generality-based aspects for each mood of the fourth figure as given in the text.

Core Idea
Each mood splits into multiple valid forms depending on the logical relations (generality/particularity) between its three terms.

After listing the five moods, the author explains that each mood can be realized in multiple 'aspects' (وجوه) based on how the minor, middle, and major terms are related in terms of generality and particularity (e.g., whether the minor is a species of the middle, or the middle is coextensive with the major, etc.). The number of these aspects varies by mood. This lesson will help you understand and count those variations.

1
Identify the three terms

List possible relations of generality
The relations between terms can be: coextensive, one is more general, one is more specific. There are four possible patterns for the first mood (minor–middle and middle–major). The author says the first mood has four aspects.
3
Count aspects for each mood
According to the passage: first mood has 4 aspects, second has 8, third has 2, fourth has 2 (or 'on the contrary' the fifth has 4). Memorize these numbers.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the first mood (mood with universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major? Actually the first mood of the fourth figure is not specified in this passage, but we know from earlier lessons that the fourth figure's moods are: first mood: universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, etc. For this lesson, we can use any mood. For instance, consider the second mood: universal negative minor and universal affirmative major. Its eight aspects correspond to different genus-species arrangements among terms like 'خلق' (creation), 'قديم' (eternal), 'حادث' (temporal).

As for the second, it is in eight aspects; as for the third, in two aspects; as for the fourth, likewise, on the contrary, the fifth: for it is in four aspects, this is with respect to generality and particularity. As for with respect to counter-change and affirmation, the aspects occurring in each mood are sixteen: eight of them are invalid and eight remain, as previously mentioned; for the minor and major may be affirmative, and may not be but rather be counter-changed. And just as they are multiple according to generality and particularity and counter-change and affirmation, so also according to otherness, such as when the minor is tripartite and the major is bipartite or tripartite, as has been known before in each figure.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It first gives an example of the fifth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, a particular negative conclusion follows. The example uses the placeholder variables C, B, A. Then it explains that each of the five moods has multiple aspects (وجوه) based on the relations of generality and particularity between the terms (minor, middle, major). For the first mood, there are four aspects; the second has eight; the third has two; the fourth (and similarly the fifth) has four. Next, it considers the aspect of whether the propositions are affirmative (محصلة) or counter-changed (معدولة, i.e., with a negated subject or predicate). Each mood then yields sixteen possible forms, of which eight are invalid and eight valid, because the minor and major can each be affirmative or counter-changed. Finally, it mentions further variations (الغير) such as the number of terms in the premises—e.g., whether the minor is tripartite (three terms) and the major bipartite (two terms) or tripartite—as established earlier for other figures. This systematic breakdown illustrates the complexity of the fourth figure's moods and the need to consider multiple logical dimensions.

Mood Variations by Counter-Change (Udul wa Tahsil)
Variations by counter-change and affirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which combinations of affirmative and counter-changed premises yield valid moods in the fourth figure.

Core Idea
For each mood, only half of the sixteen possible counter-change combinations are valid; the other half are invalid.

In addition to term relations, a syllogism's validity can be affected by whether the propositions are affirmative (محصلة) or counter-changed (معدولة) – that is, containing negated terms like 'non-animal'. The author states that for each mood, there are sixteen total possible forms when considering counter-change, but only eight are valid. This lesson will help you understand why and how to identify the valid ones.

Guided Path
1
Define affirmative and counter-changed propositions
An affirmative proposition (محصلة) has positive terms (e.g., 'Every A is B'). A counter-changed proposition (معدولة) has a negated subject or predicate (e.g., 'Every non-A is B' or 'Every A is non-B'). In the passage, the author refers to the minor and major premises being either affirmative or counter-changed.
2
List all combinations for a given mood
For each mood, the minor and major premises each can be either affirmative (M) or counter-changed (C). That yields 2x2 = 4 combinations for the premises. However, the author says there are 16 aspects total, so likely the conclusion also varies. But the text only mentions minor and major. Possibly the 16 comes from also varying the term relations? The passage seems to separate the dimensions. We'll stick to the text: 16 aspects, 8 valid.
3
Apply the validity rule
The author says eight are valid and eight invalid, as established earlier ('كما مر من قبل'). The rule is based on the principle that a counter-changed proposition can sometimes lead to an invalid syllogism if the negation disrupts the term relations. Check whether the middle term is distributed properly.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take the first mood (universal affirmative minor, universal affirmative major). An example: 'Every صلاة (prayer) is عبادة (worship)' (M) and 'Every عبادة is طاعة (obedience)' (M). Now change the minor to counter-changed: 'Every non-صلاة is عبادة'. This may not yield a valid syllogism. Try to find a valid combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objections to the Fourth Figure: A Dialectical Analysis
Author presents and refutes five objections to the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate five objections to the fourth figure's self-sufficiency and the author's refutations.

Core Idea
The fourth figure produces by itself—objections reveal deeper logical principles.

A group of logicians raised five objections against the claim that the fourth figure is productive by itself (muntij li-dhatihi). Each objection attacks a different aspect: conversion, naturalness, epistemology, necessity, and a counterexample. The author refutes the first four point by point, defending the figure's self-sufficiency. This lesson walks through the dialectical exchange and equips you to analyze similar debates in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Thesis Under Attack
Find the phrase 'منتج لذاته' (productive by itself) in the Arabic. This is the claim that the fourth figure yields its conclusion without needing external operations like conversion.
The thesis is stated implicitly: the objectors deny it.
2
Extract the Five Objections
Read the passage and list each objection in order. Notice that each begins with 'ومنها أن يقال' (and among them is that it is said).
Objections 1-4 are clearly stated; objection 5 involves a concrete example.
3
Analyze the Logical Structure of Each Objection
For each objection, identify the hidden assumption. For example, objection 1 assumes that needing conversion implies not being self-sufficient; objection 3 assumes that if the premises are known, the conclusion must be known.
4
Examine the Author's Refutation Strategy
For each of the first four objections, note how the author responds. He often distinguishes between production and explanation, redefines terms, or denies the assumption.
For objection 5, the response is incomplete; consider what the author might have said based on his strategy.
5
Assess the Overall Strength of the Defense
Evaluate whether the refutations fully answer the objections. Do they preserve the fourth figure's self-sufficiency?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه هذا الشكل من جملة ما تعرض لقدحه طائفة من المنطقيين وقالوا إنه لا ينتج بنفسه بل بقوة الشكل الأول فإن ذلك ينتج نتيجة عكسها هو النتيجة من هذا الشكل. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن لا يلزم عنه لذاته قول آخر؛ وقد كان من الوجوه المذكورة ما يدل عليه وكذلك غيرها. وذلك من وجوه أيضًا: [۱] منها أن يقال إنه لا ينتج لذاته وإلا لا يكون مفتقرا في الإنتاج إلى عكس إحدى مقدمتيه بل إلى عكس كل واحدة منهما؛ وقد كان مفتقرًا بالضرورة. [٢] ومنها أن يقال إنه من جملة ما لم يتعرضوا له من قبل لبعده عن الطبع، وبعده عن الطبع لا يكون إلا وأن لا يكون منتجا لذاته. [٣] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجا لذاته كان من اللوازم أن يلزم من العلم به العلم بالنتيجة وليس كذلك فإن من الناس من يعترف بصدق كل واحدة من مقدمتيه، ولا يعترف بصدق النتيجة بل يثبته عليه. [٤] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإن الاجتماع بين الأصغر والأكبر إما أن يكون من لوازم الاجتماع بين الصغرى والكبرى أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان من اللوازم يلزم أن يكون منتجا نتيجة كلية وإن لم يكن يمكن أن لا يكون منتجا لكن لا يكون منتجا نتيجة كلية فلا يكون منتجا. [٥] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجًا لذاته كان منتجًا لذاته في جميع الصور، وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان كاتب بالقوة، وكل كاتب بالفعل إنسان فلا يلزم منه بعض الكاتب بالقوة كاتب بالفعل؛ إذ الكاتب بالقوة هو الذي لا يكون كاتبًا بالفعل. ثم إنه إذا لم يكن منتجا لذاته لا يكون قياسًا على ما ذكرتم من التفسير. لكنا نقول في الجواب [١] عن الأول إنه لا يكون مفتقرا في الإنتاج إلى ما ذكر تم من عكس إحدى المقدمتين وغيره بل الإنتاج يكون مفتقرا في البيان إلى ذلك؛ وذلك لا يخرجه عن كونه منتجا لذاته. [٢] وعن الثاني إن بعده عن الطبع من حيث إنه منتج لذاته؛ إذ المنتج لذاته هو الذي لا يفتقر في الإنتاج إلى غيره وإنه في هذا المعنى بعيد عن الطبع. وأما ترك التعرُّض له فذلك لا لكونه بعيدا عن الطبع بل لكونه مفروعًا عنه بعد الاطلاع على ما عداه من الأشكال خصوصا على الأول. [٣] وعن الثالث إنه في حيز المنع فإن من علمه وهو منتج علم نتيجته فيلزم من العلم به العلم بالنتيجة لا محالة. ولا يظن أن من علمه دون كونه منتجًا فقد علمه بل ما علمه؛ إذ الكلام في المنتج. (۱) مفترض. وصورته ما
Then a questioner may say concerning this figure: it is among what a group of logicians have objected to, and they said it does not produce by itself but by the power of the first figure, for that (the first figure) produces a result whose converse is the result of this figure. And if it were like that, it would be necessary that no other statement follows from it by itself; and yet among the mentioned aspects there is what indicates it, and likewise others. And that is from aspects also: [1] One of them is that it is said that it does not produce by itself; otherwise, it would not be in need in production of the conversion of one of its premises but rather of the conversion of each one of them, and it is necessarily in need. [2] And one of them is that it is said that it is among what they did not discuss before because of its distance from nature, and its distance from nature necessitates that it is not productive by itself. [3] And one of them is that it is said that if it were productive by itself, then knowledge of it would necessarily entail knowledge of the conclusion, and it is not so; for there are people who acknowledge the truth of each of its premises but do not acknowledge the truth of the conclusion, rather they deny it upon him. [4] And one of them is that it is said that it is not productive by itself because the connection between the minor and major is either a necessary consequence of the connection between the minor and major premises or not; if it is a necessary consequence, then it would produce a universal conclusion, but if not, it may not produce, but it does not produce a universal conclusion, so it does not produce. [5] And one of them is that it is said that if it were productive by itself, it would be productive in all forms, and it is not so; for if we say 'Every human is a potential writer, and every actual writer is human,' then it does not follow that some potential writer is an actual writer; because the potential writer is one who is not an actual writer. Then if it is not productive by itself, it is not a syllogism according to your interpretation. But we say in response: [1] Concerning the first: it is not in need in production of what was mentioned of conversion of one premise or another; rather, production is in need in explanation of that, and that does not remove it from being productive by itself. [2] Concerning the second: its distance from nature is insofar as it is productive by itself; for that which is productive by itself is that which does not need in production to another, and it is in this sense distant from nature. As for the lack of discussion of it, that is not because of its distance from nature but because it is already covered after examining the other figures, especially the first. [3] Concerning the third: it is in the domain of denial; for whoever knows it while it is productive knows its conclusion, so knowledge of it necessarily entails knowledge of the conclusion. And let it not be thought that whoever knows it without it being productive has known it; rather, he has not known it, for the discussion is about the productive. (1) Assumed. And its form is ...
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: it presents five objections (وجوه القدح) against the fourth figure's claim to produce a conclusion by itself (لذاته), followed by the author's refutations (الجواب). The objects claim that the fourth figure only yields a conclusion through conversion to the first figure, implying it is not self-sufficient. First objection: if it were self-sufficient, it would not need conversion of its premises, but it does. Second: earlier logicians ignored it because it is unnatural, indicating it is not self-producing. Third: people accept the premises but reject the conclusion, so knowledge of the premises does not entail knowledge of the conclusion. Fourth: the connection between minor and major terms is not guaranteed; if it were, a universal conclusion would follow, but it doesn't. Fifth: a counterexample: 'Every human is a potential writer, every actual writer is human' does not yield 'Some potential writer is an actual writer' because potential and actual are mutually exclusive. The author's responses: (1) The need for conversion is only for explaining the production, not for the production itself; the syllogism still produces by itself. (2) Unnaturalness is because it is self-sufficient (not needing other figures), not because it fails to produce; it was not discussed because it is derivable from the first figure. (3) If one truly knows the premises as productive, one must know the conclusion; those who reject the conclusion have not fully grasped the premises as productive. The text ends with a footnote 'Assumed. And its form is...', suggesting the response may continue or a diagram was intended.

قدح
منتج لذاته
عكس
الاجتماع
طائفة من المنطقيين
الجواب

A) Conversion is not actually used in the fourth figure.
B) Conversion is used only in the first figure.
C) Conversion is needed for explanation, not for production.
D) The fourth figure is actually identical to the first figure.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's self-sufficiency concerns its logical form, not whether its validity requires explanation through conversion.

Then propose a refutation using the author's distinction between production and explanation.

The fifth objection presents a counterexample with 'potential writer' and 'actual writer'. Analyze the syllogism formally: major premise 'Every actual writer is human', minor premise 'Every human is a potential writer', conclusion 'Some potential writer is an actual writer'. Determine whether this conclusion follows in the fourth figure. If not, explain why the counterexample fails to disprove the fourth figure's self-sufficiency.

Analyzing Objection 3: Knowledge of Premises vs. Knowledge of Syllogism
Author refutes the third objection against self-productive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that self-productivity would require automatic knowledge of the conclusion, and evaluate the author's response that the objection confuses knowledge of premises with knowledge of the syllogism as productive.

Core Idea
Knowing premises is not knowing the syllogism as a productive whole; the latter entails the conclusion.

One objection to self-productive syllogisms is that if they were self-productive, anyone who knew the premises would automatically know the conclusion. But that's not always the case. The author argues that the objection misses a key distinction. In this lesson, you will analyze this objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional claim in the objection
The objection states: If a syllogism is self-productive, then knowledge of it entails knowledge of the conclusion. Find this conditional in the text.
The objection is: 'If it were productive by itself, it would be a necessary consequence that knowledge of it entails knowledge of the conclusion.'
2
Extract the counterexample
The objector claims: There are people who affirm both premises but deny the conclusion. This is presented as evidence that the conditional is false.
This is meant to show that knowledge of premises alone does not guarantee knowledge of conclusion.
3
Find the author's response
The author denies the counterexample applies: such people do not know the syllogism as productive; they only know the premises. Knowledge of the syllogism as a productive whole necessarily yields the conclusion.
The response distinguishes between knowing the parts (premises) and knowing the whole (the productive syllogism).
4
Evaluate the rebuttal
Assess whether the distinction is valid. Does the objection assume that 'knowing the syllogism' means 'knowing the premises'? The author redefines the object of knowledge.
If someone knows only the premises without recognizing them as a syllogism, they do not 'know the syllogism' in the relevant sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له من قبل لبعده عن الطبع، وبعده عن الطبع لا يكون إلا وأن لا يكون منتجا لذاته. [٣] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجا لذاته كان من اللوازم أن يلزم من العلم به العلم بالنتيجة وليس كذلك فإن من الناس من يعترف بصدق كل واحدة من مقدمتيه، ولا يعترف بصدق النتيجة بل يثبته(١) عليه. [٤] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإن الاجتماع بين الأصغر والأكبر إما أن يكون من لوازم الاجتماع بين الصغرى والكبرى أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان من اللوازم يلزم أن يكون منتجا نتيجة كلية وإن لم يكن يمكن أن لا يكون منتجا لكن لا يكون منتجا نتيجة كلية فلا يكون منتجا. [٥] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجًا لذاته كان منتجًا لذاته في جميع الصور، وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان كاتب بالقوة، وكل كاتب بالفعل إنسان فلا يلزم منه بعض الكاتب بالقوة كاتب بالفعل؛ إذ الكاتب بالقوة هو الذي لا يكون كاتبًا بالفعل. ثم إنه إذا لم يكن منتجا لذاته لا يكون قياسًا على ما ذكرتم من التفسير.
لكنا نقول في الجواب [١] عن الأول إنه لا يكون مفتقرا في الإنتاج إلى ما ذكرتم من عكس إحدى المقدمتين وغيره بل الإنتاج يكون مفتقرا في البيان إلى ذلك؛ وذلك لا يخرجه عن كونه منتجا لذاته. [٢] وعن الثاني إن بعده عن الطبع من حيث إنه منتج لذاته؛ إذ المنتج لذاته هو الذي لا يفتقر في الإنتاج إلى غيره وإنه في هذا المعنى بعيد عن الطبع. وأما ترك التعرُّض له فذلك لا لكونه بعيدا عن الطبع بل لكونه مفروعًا عنه بعد الاطلاع على ما عداه من الأشكال خصوصا على الأول. [٣] وعن الثالث إنه في حيز المنع فإن من علمه وهو منتج علم نتيجته فيلزم من العلم به العلم بالنتيجة لا محالة. ولا يظن أن من علمه دون كونه منتجًا فقد علمه بل ما علمه؛ إذ الكلام في المنتج. (١) مفترض. وصورته ما [٤] وعن الرابع إن الاجتماع بين الأصغر والأكبر من لوازم الاجتماع بين الصغرى والكبرى على الترتيب الذي مرَّ ذكره لكن لا يلزم منه أن يكون منتجا موجبة كلية؛ إذ كونه منتجا موجبة كلية من لوازم الاجتماع بين كل الأصغر والأكبر لا من لوازم الاجتماع بين الأصغر والأكبر مطلقا. [٥] وعن الخامس إن الصغرى فيما ذكرتم من المثال أو الكبرى فيه لا تكون صادقة وإلا لا يكون الأوسط متحدا فيه؛ والكلام فيما يكون الأوسط فيه متحدا. ثم الكلام قد تم في هذا المقام بتوفيق الله العزيز العلام.
It is said regarding its remoteness from nature, and its remoteness from nature is not except that it is not productive by itself. [3] And among them is that it is said: If it were productive by itself, it would be a necessary consequence that knowledge of it entails knowledge of the conclusion, but it is not so, for among people are those who affirm the truth of each of its two premises but do not affirm the truth of the conclusion, rather he [the objector] requires it upon him. [4] And among them is that it is said: It is not productive by itself, because the combination between the minor and major terms is either a necessary concomitant of the combination between the minor and major premises or not; if it is a necessary concomitant, then it would entail that it produce a universal conclusion, and if not, it could be non-productive, but it would not produce a universal conclusion, so it is not productive. [5] And among them is that it is said: If it were productive by itself, it would be productive by itself in all forms, but it is not so, for if we say 'Every human is potentially a writer, and every actually a writer is human', it does not entail that some potentially a writer is actually a writer, since the potentially a writer is the one who is not actually a writer. Then if it is not productive by itself, it is not a syllogism according to your interpretation. But we say in response: [1] Regarding the first: It does not depend in its production on what you mentioned of conversion of one of the two premises and other things, but rather production depends in explanation on that; and that does not remove it from being productive by itself. [2] Regarding the second: Its remoteness from nature is insofar as it is productive by itself; for the self-productive is that which does not depend in production on another, and in this sense it is remote from nature. As for refraining from addressing it, that is not because it is remote from nature, but because it is finished with after insight into the other figures, especially the first. [3] Regarding the third: It is in the realm of denial, for whoever knows it while it is productive knows its conclusion, so knowledge of it necessarily entails knowledge of the conclusion. And one should not think that whoever knows it without it being productive knows it, rather he does not know it; for the discussion is about the productive. (1) It is assumed. And its form is... [4] Regarding the fourth: The combination between the minor and major terms is a necessary concomitant of the combination between the minor and major premises according to the arrangement that has passed, but it does not entail that it produce a universal affirmative; for its being productive of a universal affirmative is a necessary concomitant of the combination between every minor and major, not of the combination between the minor and major absolutely. [5] Regarding the fifth: The minor premise in the example you mentioned or the major premise in it is not true, otherwise the middle term would not be unified in it; and the discussion concerns what has a unified middle term. Then the discussion has been completed in this place with the success of Allah the Mighty, the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: it presents three remaining objections (the first two are missing) and all five answers defending the claim that a syllogism is productive by itself (muntij bi-dhatih). The author responds to each objection in order.

The third objection argues: if a syllogism were self-productive, then knowing its premises would necessarily lead to knowing its conclusion; but people sometimes affirm the premises while denying the conclusion, so it is not self-productive. The answer denies the premise: whoever knows the syllogism as productive indeed knows its conclusion. The objection mistakenly considers knowledge of the premises alone, not of the syllogism as productive.

The fourth objection: the relation between the minor and major terms is either a necessary concomitant of the relation between the premises or not. If it is, then the conclusion is universal; if not, the syllogism may be non-productive. So it is not necessarily self-productive. The answer clarifies that the minor-major relation is a concomitant, but that does not entail producing a universal conclusion, only that the conclusion follows from that combination.

The fifth objection: if a syllogism were self-productive, it would be productive in all forms. But a counterexample is given: 'Every human is potentially a writer; every actually a writer is human' does not yield 'Some potentially a writer is actually a writer' because potentiality excludes actuality. Thus it fails. The answer points out that in this example the premises are not both true (the second premise is false) and the middle term is not unified, so it is not a valid syllogism. The discussion is restricted to valid syllogisms with a unified middle term.

The first two objections (now missing) likely concerned reliance on conversion and distance from nature. The answers explain that [1] production does not depend on conversion, but its explanation might; [2] being self-productive is naturally remote, but that does not negate it; refraining from using it is due to completion, not difficulty.

All examples are from logic (potential/actual writer), not from Islamic sciences, but the argument remains in the logical domain.

Key Terms
منتجا لذاته
مقدمتيه
نتيجة

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the third objection fail?
A) Because the premises are false
B) Because the people in the counterexample do not know the syllogism as productive
C) Because the conclusion is actually known by everyone
D) Because the syllogism is not in the first figure

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's self-productivity means its conclusion follows necessarily from its premises; knowledge of the premises as a productive whole yields knowledge of the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the fiqh argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is forbidden.' An opponent says: 'I affirm both premises but do not accept the conclusion.' How would you respond using the distinction from this lesson? Write a short rebuttal.
?
2. Find a real example from usul al-fiqh where a jurist defends a ruling by a syllogism and an opponent raises a similar objection about knowledge. Explain how the jurist could counter.

The Syllogism

Analyzing Objection 4: The Combination of Terms and Universal Conclusion
Author refutes the fourth objection against self-productive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that the combination of minor and major terms does not guarantee a universal conclusion, and evaluate the author's response that the production of a universal conclusion requires the combination of every minor and major.

Core Idea
The combination of terms in a syllogism necessarily yields a conclusion, but not necessarily a universal one.

The fourth objection argues that if the combination of minor and major terms is a necessary concomitant of the premises, then the syllogism would always produce a universal conclusion. Since not all valid syllogisms have universal conclusions, the syllogism is not self-productive. The author clarifies the scope of the necessity.

Guided Path
1
Parse the disjunctive argument in the objection
The objection presents a disjunction: the combination of terms is either a necessary concomitant of the premises or not. If it is, the conclusion must be universal; if not, the syllogism may be non-productive.
The objector aims to show that self-productivity would force universal conclusions, which is false.
2
Identify the author's distinction
The author agrees that the combination of terms is a necessary concomitant, but denies that it forces a universal conclusion. He distinguishes between the combination of the minor and major absolutely and the combination of every minor and major.
The production of a universal conclusion requires the latter, not the former.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider a syllogism with a particular conclusion. The combination of terms is necessary for a conclusion, but the conclusion may be particular if the premises are particular.
This shows that self-productivity does not entail universality.
4
Evaluate the rebuttal
Assess whether the author's distinction saves the claim. Does it properly account for syllogisms with particular conclusions?
The objection's flaw is assuming that any necessary combination yields a universal; the author shows it only yields some conclusion.

Commentary

The third objection argues: if a syllogism were self-productive, then knowing its premises would necessarily lead to knowing its conclusion; but people sometimes affirm the premises while denying the conclusion, so it is not self-productive. The answer denies the premise: whoever knows the syllogism as productive indeed knows its conclusion. The objection mistakenly considers knowledge of the premises alone, not of the syllogism as productive.

The fourth objection: the relation between the minor and major terms is either a necessary concomitant of the relation between the premises or not. If it is, then the conclusion is universal; if not, the syllogism may be non-productive. So it is not necessarily self-productive. The answer clarifies that the minor-major relation is a concomitant, but that does not entail producing a universal conclusion, only that the conclusion follows from that combination.

The fifth objection: if a syllogism were self-productive, it would be productive in all forms. But a counterexample is given: 'Every human is potentially a writer; every actually a writer is human' does not yield 'Some potentially a writer is actually a writer' because potentiality excludes actuality. Thus it fails. The answer points out that in this example the premises are not both true (the second premise is false) and the middle term is not unified, so it is not a valid syllogism. The discussion is restricted to valid syllogisms with a unified middle term.

Analyzing Objection 5: The Counterexample of Potential and Actual Writer
Author refutes the fifth objection against self-productive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the counterexample of 'Every human is potentially a writer; every actual writer is human' and evaluate the author's response that the premises are not both true or the middle term is not unified.

Core Idea
A seemingly counterproductive syllogism fails either because a premise is false or the middle term is not unified.

The fifth objection presents a specific syllogism that allegedly is not productive despite having true-sounding premises. The author argues that this example does not satisfy the conditions of a valid syllogism: either one premise is false or the middle term is not unified. This lesson dissects that counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Identify the counterexample syllogism
The objector gives: 'Every human is potentially a writer; every actually a writer is human.' The attempted conclusion is 'Some potentially a writer is actually a writer.'
This is meant to show a non-productive syllogism in a form that should be productive.
2
Check the truth of the premises
The author says the minor or major premise is not true. 'Every human is potentially a writer' might be false (not all humans have writing potential in the relevant sense). Also, 'every actually a writer is human' is true but may not be the correct major premise.
If a premise is false, the syllogism may still be valid but not sound; the objection concerns self-productivity, which requires true premises? Actually, self-productivity is about the logical form, not truth. The author seems to blend validity and soundness.
3
Examine the middle term unity
The middle term 'writer' (kaatib) is used with different meanings: 'potentially a writer' vs 'actually a writer'. The author says the middle term is not unified, so the syllogism is invalid.
A valid syllogism requires the middle term to be used in the same sense in both premises.
4
Evaluate the rebuttal
The author's response shows that the example is not a valid syllogism at all, so it does not threaten the claim that valid syllogisms are self-productive.
The objection fails because it uses an invalid form or false premises.

The answer denies the premise: whoever knows the syllogism as productive indeed knows its conclusion. The objection mistakenly considers knowledge of the premises alone, not of the syllogism as productive.

The fourth objection: the relation between the minor and major terms is either a necessary concomitant of the relation between the premises or not. If it is, then the conclusion is universal; if not, the syllogism may be non-productive. So it is not necessarily self-productive. The answer clarifies that the minor-major relation is a concomitant, but that does not entail producing a universal conclusion, only that the conclusion follows from that combination.

The fifth objection: if a syllogism were self-productive, it would be productive in all forms. But a counterexample is given: 'Every human is potentially a writer; every actually a writer is human' does not yield 'Some potentially a writer is actually a writer' because potentiality excludes actuality. Thus it fails. The answer points out that in this example the premises are not both true (the second premise is false) and the middle term is not unified, so it is not a valid syllogism. The discussion is restricted to valid syllogisms with a unified middle term.

The first two objections (now missing) likely concerned reliance on conversion and distance from nature. The answers explain that [1] production does not depend on conversion, but its explanation might; [2] being self-productive is naturally remote, but that does not negate it; refraining from using it is due to completion, not difficulty.

All examples are from logic (potential/actual writer), not from Islamic sciences, but the argument remains in the logical domain.

Key Terms
قوة وفعل
الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The premises are not in the first figure
C) Either a premise is false or the middle term is not unified
D) The syllogism does not have a universal conclusion

Key Takeaway
A valid counterexample to self-productivity must itself be a valid syllogism with a unified middle term and true premises; otherwise it does not refute the claim.

Introduction to Mixed Syllogisms and Their Enumeration
Author introduces mixed syllogisms and gives their count.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible mixed syllogisms (625) and identify which modal types are excluded.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms multiply to 625 combos, but only some yield sound proofs.

This passage opens the topic of mixed syllogisms, where premises have different modalities. The author first gives the total count of possible combinations—625—based on the proposition types he is considering, excluding specific possibility and future tense. Understanding this count is key to grasping the scope of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total number
The author states there are 625 possible mixed syllogisms. This number comes from multiplying the proposition types considered (excluding certain ones).
The exact calculation is not given, but the number is a product of premise types.
2
Note the exclusions
Specific possibility (al-imkan al-akhass) and future tense (al-istiqbali) propositions are excluded from this count.
These types are likely excluded because they complicate the modal analysis.
3
Recognize validity variance
Not all 625 combinations form valid syllogisms with true premises; some yield false conclusions or are structurally invalid.
Validity depends on the modal interaction between premises.
4
Understand conclusion modality
The conclusion's modality can follow the minor premise, the major premise, or be different from both.
This rule is general across figures, but specifics are described later.

[Second Type: On Mixed Syllogisms] Know first that the syllogisms in this type are numerous, numerous as the product of multiplying the propositions among themselves; and they are six hundred and twenty-five according to the mentioned propositions, excluding special possibility and future tense, but some of them, when mixed, form a syllogism with true premises, and some do not. And they do not differ with the difference of the moods in each figure except in the fourth figure. And the conclusion in modality may be dependent on the minor premise, may be dependent on the major premise, and may not be, rather be contrary to both; and that is not known except by reference to what produces knowledge in the four figures that have been mentioned. [1.2.4] [Chapter: On Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure] As for the first figure, we say that the mixed syllogisms in this figure are of three divisions: [A] The first: that is concerning other than the two possible propositions, I mean the general and the specific, together with other than the conditional and the conventional; so each one of those sentences, in which the minor term is actually included under the middle term, is the minor premise for each one of the sentences.

First Division of Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure
Author describes the first division of mixed syllogisms in the first figure.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The first division: premises are not both possible and not conditional, with the minor term actually included under the middle.

After introducing the overall count of mixed syllogisms, the author focuses on the first figure, dividing its mixed syllogisms into three. The first division is defined by what it excludes: it covers all mixed syllogisms except those where both premises are possible (general or specific) and those with conditional or conventional premises. The key structural feature is that the minor term is actually included under the middle term, meaning the minor premise is a categorical affirmative proposition.

Guided Path
1

The author announces that mixed syllogisms in the first figure are divided into three sections.
The passage only describes the first; the other two are presumably covered later.
2
Focus on the first division's exclusions
The first division excludes both types of possible premises (general and specific) and also excludes conditional and conventional propositions.
Thus, the premises here are from the remaining modal and non-modal types (e.g., necessary, absolute).
3
Understand 'minor term actually included'
The phrase means the minor premise is a categorical affirmative statement where the minor term is subject and the middle term is predicate, indicating actual inclusion.
This ensures the full force of the first figure: the minor term falls under the middle term.
4
Realize scope of this division
This division covers most mixed syllogisms, as it includes all combinations except those explicitly excluded.
It forms the basis for further analysis in the first figure.

Conditions for a Productive Syllogism
Author replies to two objections concerning necessity and truth in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a universal affirmative conclusion in a categorical syllogism and evaluate objections based on truth of premises and unity of the middle term.

Core Idea
Not every premise combination yields a universal affirmative; the union must be universal.

In dialectical contexts, it is common for opponents to raise objections about the necessity and truth of syllogisms. The author here responds to two such objections, clarifying that a universal affirmative conclusion requires the minor and major terms to be universally united, not merely combined. He also shows that an objection fails because its example contains a false premise, which breaks the required unity of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the response and note that the fourth objection concerns necessity of a universal affirmative conclusion, while the fifth concerns the truth of premises in an example.
2
Extract the condition for universal affirmative
The author states that producing a universal affirmative requires the union between every minor and every major (universal union), not just a partial union.
This condition ensures the conclusion follows necessarily in quantity.
3
Apply the condition to the fourth objection
The opponent assumed any union yields a universal affirmative; show that this is false unless the union is universal. Evaluate whether the given syllogism meets the condition.
4
Analyze the fifth objection
The author points out that the opponent's example has a false premise, which means the middle term is not truly unified. Without true premises and unified middle term, the syllogism is not productive.
The discussion only concerns syllogisms where the middle term is unified and premises are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] وعن الرابع إن الاجتماع بين الأصغر والأكبر من لوازم الاجتماع بين الصغرى والكبرى على الترتيب الذي مرَّ ذكره لكن لا يلزم منه أن يكون منتجا موجبة كلية؛ إذ كونه منتجا موجبة كلية من لوازم الاجتماع بين كل الأصغر والأكبر لا من لوازم الاجتماع بين الأصغر والأكبر مطلقا. [٥] وعن الخامس إن الصغرى فيما ذكرتم من المثال أو الكبرى فيه لا تكون صادقة وإلا لا يكون الأوسط متحدا فيه؛ والكلام فيما يكون الأوسط فيه متحدا. ثم الكلام قد تم في هذا المقام بتوفيق الله العزيز العلام.
[4] Concerning the fourth objection: The union between the minor and major [terms] is a necessary concomitant of the union between the minor premise and the major premise in the order previously mentioned; but it does not necessarily follow from it that it produces a universal affirmative; for its being productive of a universal affirmative is a necessary concomitant of the union between every minor and major, not a necessary concomitant of the union between the minor and major absolutely. [5] Concerning the fifth objection: The minor premise in the example you mentioned, or the major premise therein, is not true; otherwise, the middle term would not be unified in it. The discussion is about that in which the middle term is unified. Then the discussion has concluded in this place by the grace of God, the Mighty, the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to two objections raised earlier (the fourth and fifth). The author begins with the fourth objection: someone had argued that if the minor and major terms are combined in a certain way, a universal affirmative necessarily follows. The author rejects this: that combination only entails a universal affirmative if the union is between every minor and every major, not just some. In other words, the necessity of the conclusion's quantity depends on the universality of the union. Then the author answers the fifth objection: the example given by the opponent contains a premise that is false—otherwise the middle term would not be truly unified. The entire discussion is premised on the middle term being genuinely unified. He closes by stating the discussion is complete, acknowledging divine assistance.

Understanding Mixed Syllogisms: Modal Combinations
Author introduces mixed syllogisms with a modal enumeration rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mixed syllogisms, compute the number of possible combinations, and determine when the conclusion's modality follows the minor or major premise or differs.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms multiply modal propositions, but not all combinations yield valid arguments.

In this passage, the author introduces a new type of syllogism where premises have different modalities. He quantifies the possible combinations and outlines a crucial rule: the conclusion's modality may depend on either premise or be entirely different. This lays the foundation for analyzing modal reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the enumeration
The author calculates 625 combinations as 25×25, based on five main proposition types (excluding special possibility and future). This reflects the product of possible modalities for two premises.
25 comes from 5×5; the five are probably necessary, perpetual, absolute, possible, and contingent.
2
Distinguish valid from invalid
Not all 625 combinations form a valid syllogism with true premises. Some 'do not form a syllogism' – meaning they are invalid. This cues you to evaluate validity later.
The author will later specify which moods work.
3
Note the fourth figure exception
The author claims that moods do not differ by figure except in the fourth figure. This is a technical point about how moods vary across figures in mixed syllogisms.
The fourth figure has unique behavior.
4
Grasp the conclusion modality rule
The conclusion's modality (jiha) may follow the minor premise, the major premise, or be different from both. This is not arbitrary but determined by examining the four figures.
This rule is key for evaluating modal syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢.٤. ] النوع الثاني : في المختلطات واعلم أولا بأن الأقيسة في هذا النوع متعددة تعدد الحاصل من ضرب القضايا في نفسها؛ وهي ستمائة وخمسة وعشرون بحسب القضايا المذكورة دون الإمكان الأخص والاستقبالي لكن البعض منها عند الاختلاط ما ينعقد قياسًا صادق المقدّمات، والبعض منها ما لا ينعقد. وإنها لا تختلف باختلاف الضروب في كل شكل إلا في الشكل الرابع. والنتيجة في الجهة قد تكون تابعة للصغرى، وقد تكون تابعة للكبرى، وقد لا تكون بل تكون مخالفة لهما؛ وذلك لا يعرف إلا بالإشارة إلى ما يحصل به المعرفة في الأشكال الأربعة التي مر ذكرها .
[2.4] The second type: On mixed syllogisms. Know first that the syllogisms in this type are numerous, as numerous as the product of multiplying the propositions by themselves; and they are six hundred and twenty-five according to the mentioned propositions, excluding the special possibility and the future. But some of them, when mixed, form a syllogism with true premises, and some of them do not form [a valid syllogism]. And they do not differ by the difference of moods in each figure except in the fourth figure. And the conclusion in terms of modality may follow the minor premise, may follow the major premise, or may not [follow] but be different from both; and that is not known except by reference to what yields knowledge in the four figures that have been mentioned.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a classification and general rule for mixed syllogisms (al-mukhtalitat), where the premises have different modalities (e.g., necessary, absolute, possible). The author begins by stating that the number of possible combinations is huge: 625, calculated by multiplying the five main proposition types (excluding special possibility and future) by themselves (25 × 25 = 625). Not all combinations yield valid syllogisms. He then notes an exception: moods in the first three figures may sometimes differ from figure to figure, but in the fourth figure they do not differ. Finally, he explains the crucial property: the modality of the conclusion may follow the minor premise, the major premise, or be different from both. Determining which depends on examining the four figures individually, which he will do next.

The Syllogism

Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure: The Conclusion Follows the Major Premise
Author classifies mixed syllogisms in the first figure and proves conclusion follows major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct modality of the conclusion in a first-figure mixed syllogism based on the major premise's modality.

Core Idea
In the first figure, the conclusion's modality follows the major premise.

We have learned about categorical syllogisms with non-modal premises. Now we turn to mixed syllogisms, where the premises have different modalities. The key question is: which premise determines the conclusion's modality? This passage from the classical logic text addresses this specifically for the first figure, establishing a clear rule and proving it by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises and their modalities
Look at the minor premise (sughra) and major premise (kubra). Note whether each is absolute (mutlaqa) or necessary (daruriyya), or another modality. For example, 'Every J is B by necessity' and 'Every B is A with absolute generality'.
2
Locate the middle term and verify the first figure
In the first figure, the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. In the example, 'B' is the middle term. Confirm that the minor term (J) is subject of the minor premise and the major term (A) is predicate of the major premise.
3
Apply the rule: conclusion's modality matches the major premise
The author states that in these composites, the conclusion follows the major premise. That is, the modality of the conclusion (absolute, necessary, etc.) is the same as the modality of the major premise. In the example, since the major premise is absolute, the conclusion is absolute.
4
Test with the reverse case
Consider the reverse: minor premise absolute, major premise necessary. According to the rule, the conclusion should be necessary. The author confirms this: 'every J is B absolutely, every B is A necessarily, so every J is A necessarily'.
