[١.٢.٤ ] [ فصل : في المختلطات في الشكل الأول ] أما في الشكل الأول فنقول إن المختلطات في هذا الشكل على ثلاثة أقسام : [أ] أما الأول فذلك فيما عدا الممكنتين أعني العام والخاص مع ما عدا المشروطتين والعرفيتين؛ فكل واحد من تلك الجملة وهي التي يكون الأصغر فيها داخلا تحت الأوسط بالفعل هي الصغرى لكل واحدة من الجملة الأخيرة وهي التي لا يعتبر فيها دوام المحمول بدوام وصف الموضوع. فإن في هذه المركبات كانت النتيجة تابعة للكبرى لما أن الكبرى في الكل مشتملة على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر أو انسلب عنه: إما من غير بيان جهة بأن كانت مطلقة عامة، أو مع البيان بأن كانت موجهة بجهة معينة. والصغرى في الكل تدل على ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر كذلك: إما من غير بيان جهة، وإما مع البيان، فيكون الأكبر حاصلا للأصغر على الوجه المعتبر في الكبرى فتكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى كما في قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، وكل «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام فكل «ج» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر . ولأنه إذا [١٦٣) لم يصدق قولنا كل (ج) » « أ » بالإطلاق العام صدق نقيضه / وهو قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ دائما ؛ وقد صدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالإطلاق العام لما صدق قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة على ما عرف فنقول بعض «بج» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من (ج) أ » دائمًا فليس كل ب أ دائما؛ وقد كان كل «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام، هذا خلف. ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه لا يفيد إلا وأن تكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى الدائمة؛ والكلام فيه لكنا نقول: قد مرَّ من قبل ما يدل على كونها تابعة للكبرى فلا يمتنع أن تكون تابعة لها وإلا لكان ذلك الدليل يدل على حصول ما يمتنع حصوله؛ وذلك محال فلا يمتنع أن تكون تابعة إذن. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون ذلك مفيدًا لاستحالة حصول الممتنع عند حصول اللا ممتنع ; وقد كان من المحال أن يكون اللا ممتنع ممتنعا هذا إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية، والكبرى مطلقة عامة. فأما إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة والكبرى ضرورية فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فكل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا في الكبرى وكل «ب أ» فمعناه أن كل ما
[1.2.4] [Chapter: On Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure] As for the first figure, we say that mixed syllogisms in this figure are of three divisions: [A] The first is concerning other than the two possibles, i.e., the general and special, along with other than the two conditional and customary [propositions]. So each one of that group, which is where the minor [term] is actually included under the middle, is the minor premise for each one of the last group, which is where the perpetuity of the predicate is not considered by the perpetuity of the description of the subject. For in these composites, the conclusion follows the major premise because the major premise in all [cases] includes that everything to which the middle term is established, however it may be, the major term is established for it or denied from it: either without specifying modality, as when it is an absolute general [proposition], or with specification, as when it is a modalized proposition with a specific modality. And the minor premise in all [cases] indicates the establishment of the middle term for the minor similarly: either without specifying modality, or with specification. So the major term obtains for the minor in the manner considered in the major premise, and thus the conclusion follows the major premise, as in our saying: 'Every J is B by necessity, and every B is A with absolute generality, so every J is A with absolute generality,' for what has passed. And because if [163] our statement 'Every J is A with absolute generality' is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'No J is A always.' And our statement 'Some B is J with absolute generality' is true because our statement 'Every J is B by necessity' is true, as known. So we say 'Some B is J with absolute generality,' and 'No J is A always,' so 'Not every B is A always'; but it was the case that 'Every B is A with absolute generality' — this is a contradiction. Then an objector might say that this only proves that the conclusion follows the perpetual major premise, and the discussion is about that. But we say: What has passed indicates that it follows the major premise, so it is not impossible that it follow it; otherwise that proof would indicate the occurrence of what is impossible. That is absurd, so it is not impossible for it to follow. Therefore it must be that this proves the impossibility of the impossible occurring when the non-impossible occurs; and it was impossible for the non-impossible to be impossible. This is when the minor premise is necessary and the major premise is absolute general. But if the situation is reversed, as when the minor premise is absolute and the major premise is necessary, then it is said: 'Every J is B with absolute generality, and every B is A by necessity, so every J is A by necessity.' That is because when we say in the major premise 'Every B is A,' its meaning is that everything that

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of mixed syllogisms specifically in the first figure of categorical syllogisms. The author begins by stating that mixed syllogisms in the first figure fall into three divisions, but he only elaborates on the first division here. This division excludes certain proposition types (the two possibles, conditional, and customary) and focuses on propositions where the minor term is actually included under the middle term. The key claim is that in these combinations, the conclusion's modality follows the major premise. Why? Because the major premise contains the rule for the major term: it says that whatever has the middle term gets (or is denied) the major term in a certain manner, either absolutely or with a specified modality. The minor premise only establishes that the middle term holds for the minor in some manner. Therefore, the major term applies to the minor in exactly the manner specified in the major premise. The author gives an example: if the minor premise is 'Every J is B necessarily' and the major premise is 'Every B is A absolutely', the conclusion is 'Every J is A absolutely'. Then he provides a proof by contradiction (khalf): suppose the conclusion 'Every J is A absolutely' is false. Then its contradictory 'No J is A always' is true. But from the minor premise 'Every J is B necessarily', we know that some B is J absolutely (by conversion or subalternation). Then we have: some B is J absolutely, and no J is A always, so not every B is A always, contradicting the major premise 'Every B is A absolutely'. Hence the conclusion must be true. The author then addresses a potential objection: this proof only shows the conclusion follows when the major premise is perpetual (al-kubra al-da'ima) — but the major premise here is absolute, not perpetual. He responds that the proof does not rely on perpetuity; it works because the major premise is absolute. The proof shows that if the conclusion didn't follow, an impossibility would arise. He then considers the reverse case: minor absolute and major necessary, concluding that the conclusion becomes necessary. The text ends mid-sentence while explaining the meaning of 'Every B is A' in the necessary case.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Principle: Conclusion Follows Major Premise
Author establishes the principle that conclusion follows major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of a mixed modal categorical syllogism's conclusion by applying the principle that it follows the major premise.

Core Idea
The major premise dictates the conclusion's modality, not the minor.

In modal logic, syllogisms mix necessary and absolute premises. A key debate is which premise determines the conclusion's modality. This lesson presents the classical argument that the major premise is decisive, supported by a proof and a reductio ad impossibile.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's components
In the example 'Every J is B necessarily, every B is A absolutely generally', locate the minor term (J), middle term (B), major term (A), and their modalities.
The minor premise is necessary, the major is absolute.
2
Observe the conclusion's modality
The conclusion 'Every J is A absolutely generally' matches the major premise's modality (absolute), not the minor's (necessary).
3
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion is false. Then its contradictory ('No J is A always') is true. Combine with the true premises to derive a contradiction, showing the original conclusion must be true.
The proof uses the fact that some B is J (from the necessary minor) and the absolute major.
4
Generalize the principle
The author argues that because the major premise governs the relationship between middle and major terms, the conclusion's modality necessarily follows the major premise in all mixed modal syllogisms.
This holds for both affirmative and negative conclusions.

الأخيرة وهي التي لا يعتبر فيها دوام المحمول بدوام وصف الموضوع. فإن في هذه المركبات كانت النتيجة تابعة للكبرى لما أن الكبرى في الكل مشتملة على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر أو انسلب عنه: إما من غير بيان جهة بأن كانت مطلقة عامة، أو مع البيان بأن كانت موجهة بجهة معينة. والصغرى في الكل تدل على ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر كذلك: إما من غير بيان جهة، وإما مع البيان، فيكون الأكبر حاصلا للأصغر على الوجه المعتبر في الكبرى فتكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى كما في قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، وكل «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام فكل «ج» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر . ولأنه إذا لم يصدق قولنا كل (ج) « أ » بالإطلاق العام صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ دائما ؛ وقد صدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالإطلاق العام لما صدق قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة على ما عرف فنقول بعض «بج» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من (ج) أ دائمًا فليس كل ب أ دائما؛ وقد كان كل «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام، هذا خلف.
ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه لا يفيد إلا وأن تكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى الدائمة؛ والكلام فيه لكنا نقول: قد مرَّ من قبل ما يدل على كونها تابعة للكبرى فلا يمتنع أن تكون تابعة لها وإلا لكان ذلك الدليل يدل على حصول ما يمتنع حصوله؛ وذلك محال فلا يمتنع أن تكون تابعة إذن. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون ذلك مفيدًا لاستحالة حصول الممتنع عند حصول اللا ممتنع ؛ وقد كان من المحال أن يكون اللا ممتنع ممتنعا هذا إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية، والكبرى مطلقة عامة. فأما إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة والكبرى ضرورية فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فكل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا في الكبرى وكل «ب أ» فمعناه أن كل ما يطلق عليه «ب» كيف كان دائمًا أو غير دائم فهو من جملة ما يطلق عليه «أ» بالضرورة، و «ج» من جملة ما يطلق عليه «ب» بالإطلاق العام؛ فكان داخلا في الكبرى ويطلق عليه « أ ) بالضرورة فيلزم أن يكون كل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة؛ وتكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى كذلك.
ثم إنه من جملة ما وقع الخلاف فيه بين المتقدمين منهم من قال إنها أي النتيجة تابعة للصغرى في هذه الصورة، وذلك لأن الأوسط للأصغر بالإطلاق العام فيمكن أن يكون ثبوته له بالضرورة، ويمكن أن لا يكون. والأكبر لا يثبت للأصغر إلا بتوسط ثبوته للأوسط فيمكن أن يكون الأكبر للأصغر بالضرورة ويمكن أن لا يكون؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون النتيجة في الجهة تابعة للصغرى. ألا ترى أنا إذا قلنا زيد متحرك بالإطلاق العام، وكل متحرك متغير بالضرورة فلا يلزم منه قولنا زيد متغير بالضرورة بل يلزم منه زيد متغير بالإطلاق العام. لكنا نقول إنها تابعة للكبرى في هذه الضرورة لما مرَّ من قبل. ولأن الأوسط للأصغر إذا كان بالإطلاق العام يمكن أن يكون ثبوته له بالضرورة. فالصغرى تدل على ثبوته له بهذه الصفة والكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر بالضرورة؛ فبتقدير ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر بالضرورة كان ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر بالضرورة كذلك فكانت النتيجة ضرورية لا محالة. وكل ما كان ضروريًا على تقدير ممكن كان ضروريًا في نفس الأمر سواء وجد ذلك الممكن في نفس الأمر أو لم يوجد لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة كانت النتيجة كذلك: فكانت تابعة للكبرى كما مر . وأما قولهم زيد متحرك بالإطلاق العام، وكل متحرك متغير بالضرورة، فالكبرى فيه مشروطة عامة وهو قولنا وكل متحرك متغير بالضرورة ما دام متحركا وإلا لا تكون صادقة ولو كانت الكبرى مشروطة عامة ؛ وكلامنا في كونها ضرورية
The last [type], which is that in which the permanence of the predicate is not considered by the permanence of the description of the subject. For in these compound [syllogisms], the conclusion follows the major premise, because the major premise in all [cases] includes that everything to which the middle term is established, however it may be, the major term is established for it or is negated from it: either without indicating a modality, by being absolute general, or with indication, by being modalized with a specific modality. And the minor premise in all [cases] indicates the establishment of the middle term for the minor term similarly: either without indicating a modality, or with indication. So the major term obtains for the minor term in the manner considered in the major premise, so the conclusion follows the major premise, as in our saying: 'Every J is B necessarily, and every B is A absolutely generally, so every J is A absolutely generally' due to what has passed. And because if our statement 'Every J is A absolutely generally' is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'No J is A always'; and our statement 'Some B is J absolutely generally' has been true because our statement 'Every J is B necessarily' is true, as known. So we say: 'Some B J is absolutely generally, and no J is A always', so not every B is A always; and yet 'Every B is A absolutely generally' was [true]. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
Then an objector may say that it only proves that the conclusion follows the permanent major premise; and the discussion is about that. But we say: it has passed before what indicates that it follows the major premise, so it is not impossible that it follows it, otherwise that proof would indicate the occurrence of what is impossible; and that is impossible, so it is not impossible that it follows it then. Thus it follows that that is useful for the impossibility of the impossible occurring when the non-impossible occurs; and it was impossible that the non-impossible be impossible. This is if the minor premise is necessary and the major premise is absolute general. As for if the situation is reversed, such as when the minor premise is absolute and the major premise is necessary, it is said: 'Every J is B absolutely generally, and every B is A necessarily, so every J is A necessarily.' That is because when we say in the major premise 'Every B A', its meaning is that everything to which 'B' is applied, whether it is permanent or non-permanent, is among the things to which 'A' is applied necessarily, and 'J' is among the things to which 'B' is applied absolutely generally; so it falls under the major premise and 'A' is applied to it necessarily, so it follows that 'Every J is A necessarily'; and the conclusion follows the major premise likewise.
Then among the matters in which there is disagreement among the earlier scholars, some of them said that it—i.e., the conclusion—follows the minor premise in this form, because the middle term [is established] for the minor term absolutely generally, so its establishment for it can be necessary and can be not. And the major term is not established for the minor term except through the mediation of its establishment for the middle term, so the major term can be necessary for the minor term and can be not; and thus it follows that the conclusion in modality follows the minor premise. Do you not see that if we say 'Zayd is moving absolutely generally, and every moving thing is changing necessarily,' it does not follow from it our statement 'Zayd is changing necessarily,' but rather our statement 'Zayd is changing absolutely generally' follows from it. But we say that it follows the major premise in this necessity due to what has passed before. And because if the middle term is [established] for the minor term absolutely generally, its establishment for it can be necessary. So the minor premise indicates its establishment for it with this attribute, and the major premise indicates that everything to which the middle term is established, however it may be, the major term is established for it necessarily; so given the establishment of the middle term for the minor term necessarily, the establishment of the major term for the minor term is necessarily as well, so the conclusion is necessary without doubt. And everything that is necessary on the assumption of a possible [state] is necessary in itself, whether that possible [state] exists in itself or does not exist, due to the impossibility of the alteration of realities on possible assumptions; so the conclusion is like that, so it follows the major premise as has passed. And as for their statement 'Zayd is moving absolutely generally, and every moving thing is changing necessarily,' the major premise therein is conditional general, which is our statement 'And every moving thing is changing necessarily as long as it is moving,' otherwise it would not be true, even if the major premise were conditional general; and our discussion is about its being necessary.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of modal syllogisms, specifically arguing that the modality (e.g., necessity or absoluteness) of the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. The author first states the general principle: in compound modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise because the major premise specifies the modality under which the middle term's connection to the major term holds. He provides an example: if the minor premise is 'Every J is B necessarily' and the major premise is 'Every B is A absolutely generally', the conclusion is 'Every J is A absolutely generally'. He then gives a reductio proof: if the conclusion were not true absolutely, its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction with the premises.

An objector then raises a concern: perhaps this only proves the conclusion follows a permanent (دائمة) major premise. The author responds by referring to a previous proof that establishes the conclusion follows the major premise, and argues that if it were impossible, the proof would lead to an impossibility, which is absurd. Thus the conclusion must follow the major premise.

Next, the author considers the reverse case: minor premise absolute, major premise necessary. He argues that the conclusion is necessary, because the major premise 'Every B is A necessarily' means that anything that is B, whether permanent or not, is A necessarily. Since J is B absolutely, it falls under the major premise, so J is A necessarily. So the conclusion again follows the major premise.

Then he addresses a disagreement among earlier scholars: some say that in this reverse case, the conclusion follows the minor premise. Their argument is that the middle term is only absolutely connected to the minor term, so the major term's necessity for the minor term is not guaranteed. They give the example: 'Zayd is moving absolutely, and everything moving is necessarily changing' does not yield 'Zayd is necessarily changing' but only 'Zayd is changing absolutely'. The author refutes this by pointing out that the major premise in such an example must be understood as conditional general (مشروطة عامة), i.e., 'everything moving is necessarily changing as long as it is moving'. With that understanding, the conclusion's modality again follows the major premise, because if we assume the minor premise's connection could be necessary (which is possible), then the conclusion would be necessary. And if something is necessary on a possible assumption, it is necessary in reality, because realities do not change based on possible assumptions. Therefore, the conclusion follows the major premise in all cases.

Reverse Case and Conditional General Clarification
Author defends principle against objections in reverse case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate objections to the principle that conclusion follows major in the reverse case (minor absolute, major necessary) and explain the role of conditional general propositions.

Core Idea
Even when the major is necessary and the minor absolute, the conclusion is necessary if the major is conditional general.

When the minor premise is absolute and the major is necessary, some scholars argued the conclusion should be absolute. The author refutes this by clarifying that the necessary major must be understood as conditional general (e.g., 'as long as it is B'). This lesson examines the objection, the Zayd example, and the ultimate resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reverse case syllogism
In the form 'Every J is B absolutely generally, every B is A necessarily', the minor is absolute, major necessary. The opposing view claims the conclusion should be absolute.
The example uses Zayd: 'Zayd is moving absolutely, every moving thing is changing necessarily.'
2
Examine the objectors' reasoning
Objectors say that because the middle term's connection to the minor is only absolute, the major term's necessity cannot transfer. From 'Zayd is moving absolutely' and 'everything moving is necessarily changing', one concludes 'Zayd is changing absolutely', not necessarily.
3
Understand the conditional general interpretation
The author clarifies that the major premise 'every moving thing is necessarily changing' is true only under the condition 'as long as it is moving' (conditional general). With that condition, the necessity applies specifically when the moving property holds.
The major premise is 'كل متحرك متغير بالضرورة ما دام متحركا' (conditional general).
4
Apply the principle of possible necessities
Since the minor premise allows that the connection could be necessary (though it is stated as absolute), if we assume it were necessary, the conclusion would be necessary. But what is necessary on a possible assumption is necessary in reality because realities do not change with possible assumptions.
Thus the conclusion follows the major premise even in this form.

الأخيرة وهي التي لا يعتبر فيها دوام المحمول بدوام وصف الموضوع. فإن في هذه المركبات كانت النتيجة تابعة للكبرى لما أن الكبرى في الكل مشتملة على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر أو انسلب عنه: إما من غير بيان جهة بأن كانت مطلقة عامة، أو مع البيان بأن كانت موجهة بجهة معينة. والصغرى في الكل تدل على ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر كذلك: إما من غير بيان جهة، وإما مع البيان، فيكون الأكبر حاصلا للأصغر على الوجه المعتبر في الكبرى فتكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى كما في قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، وكل «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام فكل «ج» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر . ولأنه إذا لم يصدق قولنا كل (ج) « أ » بالإطلاق العام صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ دائما ؛ وقد صدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالإطلاق العام لما صدق قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة على ما عرف فنقول بعض «بج» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من (ج) أ دائمًا فليس كل ب أ دائما؛ وقد كان كل «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام، هذا خلف.
ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه لا يفيد إلا وأن تكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى الدائمة؛ والكلام فيه لكنا نقول: قد مرَّ من قبل ما يدل على كونها تابعة للكبرى فلا يمتنع أن تكون تابعة لها وإلا لكان ذلك الدليل يدل على حصول ما يمتنع حصوله؛ وذلك محال فلا يمتنع أن تكون تابعة إذن. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون ذلك مفيدًا لاستحالة حصول الممتنع عند حصول اللا ممتنع ؛ وقد كان من المحال أن يكون اللا ممتنع ممتنعا هذا إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية، والكبرى مطلقة عامة. فأما إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة والكبرى ضرورية فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وكل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة فكل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا في الكبرى وكل «ب أ» فمعناه أن كل ما يطلق عليه «ب» كيف كان دائمًا أو غير دائم فهو من جملة ما يطلق عليه «أ» بالضرورة، و «ج» من جملة ما يطلق عليه «ب» بالإطلاق العام؛ فكان داخلا في الكبرى ويطلق عليه « أ ) بالضرورة فيلزم أن يكون كل «ج» «أ» بالضرورة؛ وتكون النتيجة تابعة للكبرى كذلك.
ثم إنه من جملة ما وقع الخلاف فيه بين المتقدمين منهم من قال إنها أي النتيجة تابعة للصغرى في هذه الصورة، وذلك لأن الأوسط للأصغر بالإطلاق العام فيمكن أن يكون ثبوته له بالضرورة، ويمكن أن لا يكون. والأكبر لا يثبت للأصغر إلا بتوسط ثبوته للأوسط فيمكن أن يكون الأكبر للأصغر بالضرورة ويمكن أن لا يكون؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون النتيجة في الجهة تابعة للصغرى. ألا ترى أنا إذا قلنا زيد متحرك بالإطلاق العام، وكل متحرك متغير بالضرورة فلا يلزم منه قولنا زيد متغير بالضرورة بل يلزم منه زيد متغير بالإطلاق العام. لكنا نقول إنها تابعة للكبرى في هذه الضرورة لما مرَّ من قبل. ولأن الأوسط للأصغر إذا كان بالإطلاق العام يمكن أن يكون ثبوته له بالضرورة. فالصغرى تدل على ثبوته له بهذه الصفة والكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر بالضرورة؛ فبتقدير ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر بالضرورة كان ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر بالضرورة كذلك فكانت النتيجة ضرورية لا محالة. وكل ما كان ضروريًا على تقدير ممكن كان ضروريًا في نفس الأمر سواء وجد ذلك الممكن في نفس الأمر أو لم يوجد لاستحالة انقلاب الحقائق على تقادير ممكنة كانت النتيجة كذلك: فكانت تابعة للكبرى كما مر . وأما قولهم زيد متحرك بالإطلاق العام، وكل متحرك متغير بالضرورة، فالكبرى فيه مشروطة عامة وهو قولنا وكل متحرك متغير بالضرورة ما دام متحركا وإلا لا تكون صادقة ولو كانت الكبرى مشروطة عامة ؛ وكلامنا في كونها ضرورية
The last [type], which is that in which the permanence of the predicate is not considered by the permanence of the description of the subject. For in these compound [syllogisms], the conclusion follows the major premise, because the major premise in all [cases] includes that everything to which the middle term is established, however it may be, the major term is established for it or is negated from it: either without indicating a modality, by being absolute general, or with indication, by being modalized with a specific modality. And the minor premise in all [cases] indicates the establishment of the middle term for the minor term similarly: either without indicating a modality, or with indication. So the major term obtains for the minor term in the manner considered in the major premise, so the conclusion follows the major premise, as in our saying: 'Every J is B necessarily, and every B is A absolutely generally, so every J is A absolutely generally' due to what has passed. And because if our statement 'Every J is A absolutely generally' is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'No J is A always'; and our statement 'Some B is J absolutely generally' has been true because our statement 'Every J is B necessarily' is true, as known. So we say: 'Some B J is absolutely generally, and no J is A always', so not every B is A always; and yet 'Every B is A absolutely generally' was [true]. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
Then an objector may say that it only proves that the conclusion follows the permanent major premise; and the discussion is about that. But we say: it has passed before what indicates that it follows the major premise, so it is not impossible that it follows it, otherwise that proof would indicate the occurrence of what is impossible; and that is impossible, so it is not impossible that it follows it then. Thus it follows that that is useful for the impossibility of the impossible occurring when the non-impossible occurs; and it was impossible that the non-impossible be impossible. This is if the minor premise is necessary and the major premise is absolute general. As for if the situation is reversed, such as when the minor premise is absolute and the major premise is necessary, it is said: 'Every J is B absolutely generally, and every B is A necessarily, so every J is A necessarily.' That is because when we say in the major premise 'Every B A', its meaning is that everything to which 'B' is applied, whether it is permanent or non-permanent, is among the things to which 'A' is applied necessarily, and 'J' is among the things to which 'B' is applied absolutely generally; so it falls under the major premise and 'A' is applied to it necessarily, so it follows that 'Every J is A necessarily'; and the conclusion follows the major premise likewise.
Then among the matters in which there is disagreement among the earlier scholars, some of them said that it—i.e., the conclusion—follows the minor premise in this form, because the middle term [is established] for the minor term absolutely generally, so its establishment for it can be necessary and can be not. And the major term is not established for the minor term except through the mediation of its establishment for the middle term, so the major term can be necessary for the minor term and can be not; and thus it follows that the conclusion in modality follows the minor premise. Do you not see that if we say 'Zayd is moving absolutely generally, and every moving thing is changing necessarily,' it does not follow from it our statement 'Zayd is changing necessarily,' but rather our statement 'Zayd is changing absolutely generally' follows from it. But we say that it follows the major premise in this necessity due to what has passed before. And because if the middle term is [established] for the minor term absolutely generally, its establishment for it can be necessary. So the minor premise indicates its establishment for it with this attribute, and the major premise indicates that everything to which the middle term is established, however it may be, the major term is established for it necessarily; so given the establishment of the middle term for the minor term necessarily, the establishment of the major term for the minor term is necessarily as well, so the conclusion is necessary without doubt. And everything that is necessary on the assumption of a possible [state] is necessary in itself, whether that possible [state] exists in itself or does not exist, due to the impossibility of the alteration of realities on possible assumptions; so the conclusion is like that, so it follows the major premise as has passed. And as for their statement 'Zayd is moving absolutely generally, and every moving thing is changing necessarily,' the major premise therein is conditional general, which is our statement 'And every moving thing is changing necessarily as long as it is moving,' otherwise it would not be true, even if the major premise were conditional general; and our discussion is about its being necessary.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of modal syllogisms, specifically arguing that the modality (e.g., necessity or absoluteness) of the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. The author first states the general principle: in compound modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise because the major premise specifies the modality under which the middle term's connection to the major term holds. He provides an example: if the minor premise is 'Every J is B necessarily' and the major premise is 'Every B is A absolutely generally', the conclusion is 'Every J is A absolutely generally'. He then gives a reductio proof: if the conclusion were not true absolutely, its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction with the premises.

An objector then raises a concern: perhaps this only proves the conclusion follows a permanent (دائمة) major premise. The author responds by referring to a previous proof that establishes the conclusion follows the major premise, and argues that if it were impossible, the proof would lead to an impossibility, which is absurd. Thus the conclusion must follow the major premise.

Next, the author considers the reverse case: minor premise absolute, major premise necessary. He argues that the conclusion is necessary, because the major premise 'Every B is A necessarily' means that anything that is B, whether permanent or not, is A necessarily. Since J is B absolutely, it falls under the major premise, so J is A necessarily. So the conclusion again follows the major premise.

Then he addresses a disagreement among earlier scholars: some say that in this reverse case, the conclusion follows the minor premise. Their argument is that the middle term is only absolutely connected to the minor term, so the major term's necessity for the minor term is not guaranteed. They give the example: 'Zayd is moving absolutely, and everything moving is necessarily changing' does not yield 'Zayd is necessarily changing' but only 'Zayd is changing absolutely'. The author refutes this by pointing out that the major premise in such an example must be understood as conditional general (مشروطة عامة), i.e., 'everything moving is necessarily changing as long as it is moving'. With that understanding, the conclusion's modality again follows the major premise, because if we assume the minor premise's connection could be necessary (which is possible), then the conclusion would be necessary. And if something is necessary on a possible assumption, it is necessary in reality, because realities do not change based on possible assumptions. Therefore, the conclusion follows the major premise in all cases.

مطلقة عامة

Key Takeaway
A necessary major premise in a mixed modal syllogism often conceals a conditional condition, and understanding this is key to determining the conclusion's modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

Modal Conclusion in First-Figure Syllogism: Absolute Minor, Necessary Major
Author argues that conclusion's necessity derives from major premise in first figure with absolute minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure categorical syllogism where the minor premise is absolute and the major premise is necessary, and refute the objection that the conclusion should follow the minor.

Core Idea
The conclusion's necessity derives from the major premise, even if the minor is unmodalized.

This passage addresses a classical debate in modal syllogistic: when the minor premise is absolute (unmodalized) and the major is necessary, does the conclusion inherit the modality of the major or the minor? The author argues that the conclusion is necessary, following the major. He presents the opposing view and its counterexample, then refutes it by clarifying the nature of necessity. Understanding this rule is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where necessary and contingent propositions interact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's figure and components
Recognize that the passage discusses a first-figure syllogism with terms A (major), B (middle), and C (minor). The major premise is 'Every B is A necessarily' and the minor is 'Every C is B absolutely'.
The first figure requires the middle to be subject of major and predicate of minor.
2
Understand the claim: conclusion follows the major
The author asserts that because C is absolutely B, and every B is necessarily A, then C is necessarily A. The necessity of the major transfers to the conclusion regardless of the minor's modality.
This is a departure from the view that the conclusion's modality matches the weaker premise.
3
Analyze the opposing view and its example
Earlier logicians argued that the conclusion should follow the minor. Their example: 'Zayd is moving absolutely; every moving thing changes necessarily.' They claim this yields 'Zayd changes absolutely' not 'necessarily'.
This example seems to contradict the author's claim.
4
Examine the author's refutation
The author explains that the major premise in the opposing example is actually a universal conditional ('as long as it is moving'), not an absolute necessity. His discussion concerns absolute necessity, so the example is irrelevant.
Thus the conclusion's necessity stands if the major is absolute.
5
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Test the rule with a fiqh syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is necessarily forbidden. This drink is intoxicant (absolutely). Therefore, this drink is necessarily forbidden.' The conclusion follows the major.
Islamic law often uses such modal syllogisms to derive rulings.

It is said of it 'B', whether it is permanent or not, that it is part of what 'A' is applied to necessarily, and 'J' is part of what 'B' is applied to by absolute application; so it enters the major premise and 'A' is applied to it necessarily, so it follows that every 'J' is 'A' necessarily; and the conclusion follows the major premise accordingly. Then it is among what the earlier scholars disagreed about: some of them said that the conclusion follows the minor premise in this form, because the middle term belongs to the minor by absolute application, so it is possible that its existence for it is necessary, and possible that it is not. And the major is not affirmed of the minor except through the mediation of its being affirmed of the middle, so it is possible that the major is necessary for the minor and possible that it is not; then it follows that the conclusion in modality follows the minor premise. Do you not see that if we say 'Zayd is moving by absolute application, and every moving thing is changing necessarily', it does not follow from that our statement 'Zayd is changing necessarily', but rather 'Zayd is changing by absolute application'. But we say it follows the major premise in this necessity for what has preceded. And because if the middle term belongs to the minor by absolute application, it is possible that its existence for it is necessary. The minor premise indicates its existence for it with this attribute, and the major indicates that everything to which the middle is attributed, however it is, the major is attributed to it necessarily; so given the attribution of the middle to the minor necessarily, then the attribution of the major to the minor is also necessary, so the conclusion is necessarily necessary without exception. As for their statement 'Zayd is moving by absolute application, and every moving thing is changing necessarily', the major therein is a universal conditional, which is our statement 'every moving thing is changing necessarily as long as it is moving'; otherwise it would not be true, even if the major is universal conditional; and our discussion is about it being absolute necessary, so that does not invalidate what we are discussing.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on a debated modal syllogistic issue: when the minor premise is absolute (unmodalized) and the major premise is necessary, does the conclusion follow the minor in modality (being absolute) or the major (being necessary)? The author first presents the argument that the conclusion follows the major, using a schematic example with terms A, B, J. The reasoning is that if J is absolutely B, then J falls under the major premise which states that whatever is B is necessarily A, so J is necessarily A. Then the author presents the opposing view from earlier logicians: they argue that because the middle term is attributed to the minor only absolutely, the necessity of the major does not transfer; it is possible that the middle is necessary for the minor but also possible that it is not, so the conclusion's modality is determined by the minor. An example is given: 'Zayd is moving absolutely, and every moving thing changes necessarily' does not yield 'Zayd changes necessarily' but only 'Zayd changes absolutely'. The author then refutes this by asserting that the conclusion indeed follows the major. The reason is that even though the minor is absolute, it is possible that the middle is necessary for the minor; the minor shows that the middle holds for the minor in some way (possibly necessary), and the major states that whatever has the middle (however it is) has the major necessarily. So if the middle is necessary for the minor, then the major is also necessary—and since this is a possible assumption, the necessity is actual because truth does not vary with possible assumptions. The author also addresses the counterexample of Zayd by noting that the major premise there must be understood as a universal conditional ('as long as it is moving'), not an absolute necessity, which is why the example fails. The author's discussion concerns absolute necessity, so the objection is not relevant. Thus the passage defends the position that in this figure, the conclusion's necessity derives from the major premise.

D) Because the syllogism is in the second figure.

Key Takeaway
In a first-figure syllogism with an absolute minor and a necessary major, the conclusion is necessary, provided the major is absolute necessity, not conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Everything temporally originated is necessarily created. Therefore, the world is necessarily created.' Explain why the conclusion is necessary.

Modal Syllogisms: Necessary Minor and Permanent Major
Presents two views and a critique on modal syllogistic conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the two reductio arguments for the modal syllogism with necessary minor and permanent negative major, and explain why the author concludes that the conclusion can only be permanent, not necessary.

Core Idea
A necessary minor does not guarantee a necessary conclusion when the major is merely permanent.

This passage continues the exploration of modal syllogistic disputes, focusing on a specific case: the minor premise is necessary and the major is a universal negative permanent statement. Two opposing views are presented, each with a reductio proof. The first argues the conclusion follows the major (so the conclusion is permanent), the second argues it follows the minor (so the conclusion is necessary). The author then provides a critical response, arguing that the conclusion cannot be necessary because the major term is only permanent for the middle, and permanence does not entail necessity. Let's walk through these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the modal configuration
Identify that the minor premise is 'Every J is B necessarily' (ضرورية) and the major is 'Nothing that is B is A permanently' (سالبة دائمة). The conclusion is in dispute: is it 'Nothing that is J is A necessarily' or 'Nothing that is J is A permanently'?
2
Reconstruct the first view (conclusion follows the major)
The first group claims the conclusion is permanent. Their reductio: assume the opposite (some J is A absolutely possible). Combine with the major (nothing A is B permanent) to derive a contradiction with the minor (every J is B necessary). Write out each step.
This uses the impossibility of some J being B not permanently, given the minor's necessity.
3
Reconstruct the second view (conclusion follows the minor)
The second group claims the conclusion is necessary. Their reductio: assume the opposite (some J is A absolutely possible). Combine with the major to derive a contradiction with the minor's necessity. Note the difference in modality (absolute possibility vs absolute application).
The two views differ in the modality of the contradictory assumption.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author points out that the conclusion cannot be necessary because the major is only permanent for the middle term, not necessary. Even if the middle is necessary for the minor, the transfer of modality through the middle yields only permanence, since permanent is more general than necessary. Therefore, the conclusion must be permanent, not necessary.
The author sides with the first view, but adds a deeper modal analysis.
5
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Replace the variables: let J = created things, B = contingent, A = having a creator. Minor: 'Every created thing is necessarily contingent' (ضروري). Major: 'Nothing that is contingent is permanently without a creator' (دائم). Conclusion: 'No created thing is permanently without a creator' (دائمة), not 'necessarily without a creator'.
This shows the practical implication in Kalam arguments about creation.

And some of them said it follows the minor in this form: if we say 'Every J is B necessarily, and nothing that is B is A permanently', it follows that nothing that is J is A necessarily, otherwise its opposite would be true, namely our statement 'Some J is A by absolute possibility', and our statement 'Nothing that is A is B permanently' is true. Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of modal syllogistic disputes, now examining the case where the minor premise is necessary and the major premise is a universal negative (saliba da'ima). Two opposing views are presented, each with a reductio ad absurdum argument. The first view claims the conclusion follows the major premise: given 'Every J is B necessarily' and 'Nothing that is B is A permanently', a contradiction arises if we assume otherwise. The second view claims the conclusion follows the minor: assuming the conclusion is necessary leads to a contradiction with the major. The author then provides a critical response: he points out that according to some logicians, a possible minor with a permanent negative major yields no conclusion in this figure, especially for those who hold that the conclusion in this form follows the major. The reason given is that it is impossible for something to be necessary while also possibly being not necessary; therefore, the conclusion cannot simultaneously follow both premises. Furthermore, the author explains that although the middle term is necessary for the minor, the major is not necessary for the middle but merely permanent (always true). Since 'permanent' is more general than 'necessary', the major's permanence does not entail necessity when transferred to the minor. Thus the conclusion would only be permanent, not necessary, but the passage cuts off mid-sentence. The author seems to be critiquing the assumption that the conclusion must be necessary in this configuration.

B) Because the middle term cannot mediate the transfer of modality.
C) Because the conclusion must always follow the weaker premise.
D) Because this syllogism is invalid in the first figure.

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogistics, necessity does not automatically transfer from the minor through a permanent major; the conclusion's modality is determined by the more general modality of the premises.

The Syllogism

Modal Syllogism: The Possible Minor with Negative Always Major
Author presents two opposing views and argues against a necessary conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the modal combination of a possible minor and negative always major, and determine why a necessary conclusion is impossible.

Core Idea
Even if the middle term is necessary for the minor, if the major is only always, the conclusion cannot be necessary.

This passage addresses a disputed modal syllogism: a minor premise expressing possibility and a major premise that is negative and expresses permanence ('always'). Two competing views are presented, followed by a decisive critique explaining the impossibility of a necessary conclusion. We will walk through the reasoning step by step, reconstruct the arguments, and understand the general principle that the conclusion takes the modality of the major.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
The first group claims the conclusion follows the major premise in modality, so the conclusion is 'always' (dā'ima). The second group claims the conclusion follows the minor premise, so it is 'necessary' (ḍarūriyya).
2
Reconstruct the first view's argument
From 'Every J is B necessarily' and 'No B is A always', they derive 'No J is A always'. They use a contradiction to show that 'No J is A necessarily' is not forced.
This view holds that the conclusion must match the major's modality (always), not the minor's necessity.
3
Reconstruct the second view's argument
From the same premises, they try to derive 'No J is A necessarily', also using a contradiction proof. They argue that the conclusion should be necessary because the middle is necessary for the minor.
This view gives priority to the minor's modality.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author argues that the possible minor with negative always major does not produce a necessary conclusion. Even if the middle is necessary for the minor, the major is only always, and 'always' is more general than 'necessary'. Truth of 'always' does not entail truth of 'necessary'. The example of Zayd being Habashi necessarily but not necessarily white illustrates this.
5
Understand the general principle
The author concludes that in all compositions, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. If the major is always, the conclusion is always; if the major is existential and not necessary, the conclusion is not necessary; similarly for temporal or widespread modalities.
This principle has an exception: the necessary minor with negative always major, which is discussed elsewhere.

Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of modal syllogisms, specifically addressing a controversial combination: a minor premise that is possible (mumkina) and a major premise that is negative and always (saliba da'ima). The author begins by noting that this combination is a point of disagreement, similar to the case of a necessary minor with a negative always major. Two opposing views are presented. The first view holds that the conclusion follows the major premise: from 'Every J is B necessarily' and 'No B is A always', one can derive 'No J is A always' (but not 'necessarily'). However, if one tries to force a necessary conclusion, a contradiction arises. The second view holds that the conclusion follows the minor premise, attempting to derive 'No J is A necessarily' instead. This also leads to contradiction. The author then offers a critique: the possible minor with the negative always major does not produce a conclusion for some in this particular figure, especially for those who hold that the conclusion follows the major. The impossibility of a necessary conclusion is argued because the middle term (B) may be necessary for the minor (J), but the major (A) is only always (da'iman) for the middle, not necessary. Since 'always' is more general than 'necessary', the truth of a universal affirmative 'always' does not imply necessity. An example is given: 'Zayd is Habashi necessarily' (all Habashis are black by definition?), and 'No Habashi is white always' (because white is never true of Habashis), so 'Zayd is not white always' – but this conclusion is not necessary because Zayd's being black or white is not necessary (he could be an exception? Actually the example is meant to show non-necessity). The author concludes that the disagreement hinges on whether this combination yields a conclusion at all, and discusses that the conclusion in most cases follows the major premise, except when the minor is necessary and the major is negative always – that exception is noted. Finally, a general rule is stated: in all compositions, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. If the major is always, the conclusion is always; if the major is existential and not necessary, the conclusion is not necessary; and similarly for temporal or widespread modalities. The author emphasizes that the minor premise establishes the middle term for the minor term, and the major premise establishes that whatever has the middle has the major in a certain manner, so the conclusion should reflect the manner of the major.

According to the passage, why is a necessary conclusion impossible when the minor is possible and the major is negative always?
A) Because the major premise is only always, and 'always' is more general than 'necessary', so necessity does not follow.
B) Because the minor premise is possible, which contradicts necessity by definition.
C) Because the first figure is invalid for modal syllogisms.
D) Because the conclusion always follows the minor premise, which is possible.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise; a necessary conclusion requires both premises to be necessary, otherwise only a weaker modality like 'always' follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Conclusion Follows Major with Exceptions
Author analyzes mixed modal syllogisms and their conclusion mode

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rule for modal following in mixed categorical syllogisms and recognize the exception for necessary minor with absolute negative major, as well as the exclusion of conditional and 'urfiyya premises.

Core Idea
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion generally takes the mode of the major premise, but not when conditional or 'urfiyya premises are involved.

In the Islamic logical tradition, mixed modal syllogisms combine premises with different modal qualifiers (necessary, absolute, possible). This passage from a classical manual lays down a key principle: outside conditional and 'urfiyya propositions, the conclusion follows the mode of the major premise. However, there is a notable exception when the minor is necessary and the major is a negative absolute. We will unpack this rule and its subtleties, including how to spot a disguised conditional major.

Guided Path
1
Restate the General Rule
Read the author's statement: 'in what excludes the two possible premises along with what excludes the two conditional and two 'urfiyya premises, the conclusion follows the major.' This means: when neither premise is possible, conditional, or 'urfiyya, the conclusion's mode is the same as the major's mode.
The exceptions are 'possible' (mumkin) premises, 'mashruta' (conditional), and ''urfiyya 'amma' (universal conditional).
2
Identify the Exception
The author says 'except in the necessary minor with the negative absolute major.' Find this exception: when the minor is necessary (daruri) and the major is a universal negative without modality (saliba da'ima), the conclusion does not become necessary or absolute? Actually, the conclusion in that case is not necessary. Recognize that this is the only exception within the non-conditional set.
The example given: Zaid is necessarily Ethiopian; no Ethiopian is always white → conclusion: Zaid is not always white (not necessary).
3
Analyze the Rationale
Why does the exception occur? The author argues that if the major is absolute (da'im) and the minor is necessary, the predicate's absoluteness does not transfer because the necessary subject's connection to the predicate is not forced to be absolute. The middle term is established for the subject necessarily, but the major only says the predicate belongs to the middle absolutely, so the conclusion is absolute, not necessary.
This highlights the interplay of modalities: necessity in the minor does not upgrade the conclusion beyond the major's mode.
4
Examine the Apparent Counterexample
The author presents: 'Every human is not always sleeping; every sleeping is always not standing.' If the rule were unqualified, the conclusion would be 'Every human is always not standing', but it does not follow. The author resolves this by noting that the major 'every sleeping is always not standing' is actually a universal 'urfiyya (conditional) proposition, not a simple absolute. Since 'urfiyya premises are excluded, this example does not break the rule.
This teaches you to check if the major actually has a conditional sense; if it does, the rule does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

دائما فزيد ليس بأبيض دائمًا والحق هذا فإن كونه أسود أو أبيض لا يكون ضروريًا. وبالجملة فالظاهر أن الاختلاف فيه إنما وقع بحسب الاختلاف في ذلك وهو أن تكون الصغرى الممكنة مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة هل تنتج أم لا؛ والكلام فيه يعرف من بعد. ثمَّ النَّتج مختلفة في هذا المقام بناء على هذه الاختلاف ففي البعض منها مع الاستثناء وهو أن النتيجة فيما عدا الممكنتين مع ما عدا المشروطتين والعرفيتين تابعة للكبرى إلا في الصغرى الضرورية مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة. وفي البعض منها لا مع الاستثناء؛ وهذا هو الأقرب إلى الفهم. ثم الذي مرَّ ذكره فيما مرَّ من التركيبات فذلك كما يدل على أن النتيجة تابعة للكبرى في تلك الصور فكذلك في غير تلك الصور من هذا القسم حتى إذا كانت الكبرى دائمة؛ والصغرى أي قضية كانت من جملة ما عدا الممكنتين كانت النتيجة دائمة فيقال كل (ج) (ب) بالإطلاق العام، وكل ب أ دائما فكل (ج) أ دائما. وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى وجودية لا ضرورية كما يقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وكل ب أ لا بالضرورة فكل (ج) أ لا بالضرورة. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى وجودية لا دائمة أو وقتية أو منتشرة؛ والصغرى أي قضية كانت من تلك الجملة؛ إذ الصغرى في الكل تدل على ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر: إما مطلقا، وإما مع جهة معينة كما مر . والكبرى تدل على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر إما مطلقا، وإما مع جهة معينة كذلك. ولو كان كذلك لكان اللازم ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر على الوجه المعتبر في الكبرى فتكون النتيجة تابعة لها. ولا يبعد أن يقال لو كان كما ذكر تم لكانت النتيجة في الصغرى اللا دائمة مع الكبرى الدائمة تابعة للكبرى؛ وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان نائم لا دائما، وكل نائم غير قائم دائمًا فلا يلزم قولنا فكل إنسان غير قائم دائما. وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة فإنه لا يلزم قولنا ولا شيء من الإنسان بقائم دائما. غير أنه لا يكون واردا فإن الكبرى في هاتين الصورتين؛ وفيما هو بمثلهما عرفية عامة في الحقيقة وإلا لكانت الكبرى كاذبة. ولما كانت الكبرى عرفية عامة؛ وكلامنا فيما عدا المشروطتين والعرفيتين: فالكبرى فيما نحن فيه لا يمكن أن تكون من المشروطتين أو من العرفيتين أصلا.
The sum of what can possibly not be necessary cannot be necessary. Do you not see that if the major is always to the middle but not necessary, then what follows from it is not necessary, as in our statement: 'Zaid is Ethiopian by necessity, and none of the Ethiopians is white always', so Zaid is not white always. And the truth is this, for his being black or white is not necessary. In summary, it is apparent that the disagreement about it only occurred according to the disagreement about that, which is whether the possible minor with the negative absolute major yields a conclusion; and the discussion about it is known later. Then the conclusions vary in this context based on these disagreements. In some of them with an exception, which is that the conclusion, in what excludes the two possible premises along with what excludes the two conditional and two 'urfiyya premises, follows the major except in the necessary minor with the negative absolute major. And in some of them without exception; and this is closest to understanding. Then what was mentioned before of the compositions indicates that the conclusion follows the major in those forms, and likewise in other forms of this division. So if the major is absolute, and the minor is any proposition from the set excluding the two possible, then the conclusion is absolute, so it is said: 'Every J is B absolutely, and every B is A always, so every J is A always.' Similarly, if the major is existential and not necessary, as is said: 'Every J is B absolutely, and every B is A not necessarily, so every J is A not necessarily.' And likewise if the major is existential, not absolute, or temporal, or widespread, and the minor is any proposition from that set; for the minor in all cases indicates the subsistence of the middle for the minor: either absolutely, or with a specific mode as mentioned. And the major indicates that everything to which the middle is established, in whatever manner, the major is established for it, either absolutely or with a specific mode similarly. And if that were the case, then the necessary consequence would be the subsistence of the major for the minor in the manner considered in the major, so the conclusion follows it. It is not far-fetched to say that if it were as mentioned, the conclusion in the non-absolute minor with the absolute major would follow the major; but it is not so, for if we say: 'Every human is not always sleeping, and every sleeping is always not standing', then our statement does not follow: 'So every human is always not standing.' Similarly, if the major is negative, then our statement does not follow: 'No human is always standing.' However, it does not apply, for the major in these two forms, and in what is like them, is actually a universal 'urfiyya; otherwise the major would be false. Since the major is a universal 'urfiyya, and our discussion is about what excludes the two conditional and two 'urfiyya premises, the major in our case cannot be from the two conditional or the two 'urfiyya at all.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the behavior of conclusions in mixed modal syllogisms. The author begins by stating a general principle: something that can possibly not be necessary can never be necessary. He then provides an example: if 'Zaid is Ethiopian by necessity' and 'No Ethiopian is white always', it does not follow that 'Zaid is not white always' — because the conclusion is not necessary, reflecting the modal asymmetry. The author then notes that the central issue is whether a possible minor with a negative absolute major yields a conclusion; this is deferred to later. He then presents the key rule: in mixed syllogisms where both premises exclude possible, conditional, and 'urfiyya premises, the conclusion generally follows the mode of the major premise — except in one case: when the minor is necessary and the major is negative absolute. The author explains the rationale: in such cases, the conclusion deviates because a necessary subject with a negative absolute predicate does not force the absolute negative. Next, he elaborates on the rule with examples: if the major is absolute, the conclusion is absolute; if the major is existential non-necessary, the conclusion is non-necessary; and similar for temporal or widespread majors. The reasoning is that the minor establishes the middle for the subject, and the major establishes the predicate for the middle; thus the predicate attaches to the subject in the same mode as the major. The author then addresses a potential counterexample: 'Every human is not always sleeping; every sleeping is always not standing'. This would seem to violate the rule, but the author explains that the major 'every sleeping is always not standing' is actually a universal 'urfiyya (conditional) proposition, not a simple absolute. Since the rule explicitly excludes conditional and 'urfiyya premises, the counterexample is not valid. Therefore, the initial rule stands.

Key Terms
ضروري (daruri)
دائم (da'im)
ممكن (mumkin)
كبرى (kubra)
صغرى (sughra)
نتيجة (natija)
تابعة (tabi'a)
عرفية عامة ('urfiyya 'amma)
مشروطة (mashruta)

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the exception to the rule that in mixed modal syllogisms (outside possible, conditional, and 'urfiyya premises) the conclusion follows the major?
A) When the minor is possible and the major is absolute.
B) When the minor is necessary and the major is negative absolute.
C) When the minor is temporal and the major is necessary.
D) When the major is conditional and the minor is absolute.

Key Takeaway
Modal following in mixed syllogisms is generally determined by the major premise, but one must exclude conditional and 'urfiyya premises and note the exception when a necessary minor pairs with a negative absolute major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: When the Major is Conditional
Author discusses mixed syllogisms where the minor is necessary, absolute, or conditional and the major is conditional with universal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed syllogism where the major premise is a universal conditional and the minor is necessary, absolute, or conditional.

Core Idea
In mixed modals with a conditional major, the conclusion follows the minor, not the major.

This passage explores a specific type of mixed modal syllogism where the major is a universal conditional (mashruta 'amma) and the minor comes from the set of non-possible premises (necessary, absolute, or conditional). The key rule is that the conclusion takes the modality of the minor premise. This is crucial for correctly evaluating arguments that combine necessary and conditional statements, common in Islamic theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
Locate the two premises in the Arabic. The minor is the one with the subject of the conclusion, and the major contains the predicate and the middle term. In the example, the minor is 'كل ج ب' with necessity, and the major is 'كل ب أ ما دام ب' with necessity and condition.
The Arabic variables are ج (j), ب (b), أ (a).
2
Determine the modality of the minor
Check whether the minor is necessary (daruri), absolute (da'im), or conditional (mashruta). The passage considers each case separately. For instance, if the minor is 'كل ج ب بالضرورة', it is necessary.
3
Recognize that the major is a universal conditional
The major is described as 'مشروطة عامة'. This means the predicate holds for the subject only as long as the subject exists under a certain description (e.g., 'as long as it is B').
The condition is attached to the middle term.
4
Apply the rule: conclusion follows the minor's modality
The author states that when the minor is necessary, the conclusion is also necessary. When the minor is absolute, the conclusion is absolute. When the minor is conditional, the conclusion follows the minor. Verify with the example: necessary minor gives necessary conclusion.
This is because the major is necessary for the middle, but the middle's relation to the subject determines the conclusion's modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني من الأقسام فذلك من المختلطات ما يكون فيما عدا الممكنتين مع المشروطتين والوقتيتين المشهورتين كل واحدة من الجملة الأولى هي الصغرى لكل واحدة من الجملة الأخيرة. وفي هذا القسم من المباحث الأول هو أن تكون الكبرى مشروطة عامة لكل واحدة من الجملة الأولى. فالصغرى إذا كانت ضرورية كانت النتيجة تابعة للصغرى لما أن الأكبر ضروري لوصف الأوسط الضروري لذات الأصغر فيقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب»، وبالضرورة كل ب أ ما دام «ب» فبالضرورة كل «ج» «أ». وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى دائمة؛ إذ النتيجة تابعة للصغرى أيضًا؛ وذلك لأن الأكبر وإن كان ضروريا للأوسط فلا ندري أن الأوسط ضروري للأصغر. أم غير ضروري فلا ندري إذن أن الأكبر ضروري للأصغر أم غير ضروري. غير أنه دائم على تقديري كونه ضروريًا للأصغر وعدم كونه ضروريا لما أن الأصغر لا يحصل إلا عند حصول الأوسط فتكون النتيجة دائمة فكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى مشروطة عامة. فإن النتيجة تابعة للصغرى والكبرى كذلك لما أن الأكبر ضروري للأوسط الضروري لوصف الأصغر فيقال بالضرورة
As for the second of the divisions, that is from the mixed [syllogisms] what occurs in what excludes the two possible premises along with the two conditional and two temporal well-known premises; each one of the first set is the minor for each one of the last set. In this division, the first investigation is that the major is a universal conditional for each one of the first set. Thus, if the minor is necessary, the conclusion follows the minor, because the major is necessary for the description of the middle which is necessary for the essence of the minor. So it is said by necessity: 'Every J is B', and by necessity: 'Every B is A as long as it is B', so by necessity: 'Every J is A'. Similarly, if the minor is absolute, for the conclusion also follows the minor; and that is because the major, even if it is necessary for the middle, we do not know if the middle is necessary for the minor or not necessary, so we do not know if the major is necessary for the minor or not. However, it is absolute in both cases of its being necessary for the minor and its not being necessary, because the minor does not occur except when the middle occurs. So the conclusion is absolute. Likewise, if the minor is a universal conditional, for the conclusion follows the minor and the major similarly, because the major is necessary for the middle which is necessary for the description of the minor. So it is said by necessity...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage introduces the second division of mixed modal syllogisms, where the premises exclude possible propositions and instead involve conditional and temporal premises. The author states that in this division, each premise from the first set (non-possible) serves as the minor for each premise from the second set (conditional/temporal). The first investigation concerns cases where the major is a universal conditional ('mashruta 'amma'). When the minor is necessary, the conclusion follows the minor because the major (the predicate) is necessary for the middle term which is itself necessary for the subject of the minor. An example is given: 'Every J is B by necessity, and every B is A as long as it is B by necessity, therefore every J is A by necessity.' The conclusion retains the necessity of the minor. When the minor is absolute (da'im), the conclusion also follows the minor, because although the major is necessary for the middle, the modality of the middle's relationship to the subject is unknown (it could be necessary or not), so the conclusion reverts to absolute — i.e., the predicate holds always but not necessarily. This is because the subject (asghar) only exists when the middle term exists, so the predicate's attachment is continuous but not strictly necessary. Finally, if the minor itself is a universal conditional, the conclusion follows both the minor and the major, essentially maintaining the conditional structure. The author's main point is that in this division, the conclusion follows the minor premise, unlike the first division where it followed the major.

مشروطة عامة
وقتية مشهورة
مختلطات
تابعة للصغرى

Checkpoint

A) Absolute (da'im) because the major is conditional.
B) Necessary (daruri) because the conclusion follows the minor.
C) Possible (mumkin) because of the conditional major.
D) Temporal (waqti) because of the temporal aspect.

Key Takeaway
In mixed syllogisms with a conditional major, the conclusion's modality is determined by the minor premise.

Therefore, every created thing is non-eternal. Is the conclusion necessary or absolute? Justify your answer using the text's reasoning.
