للأوسط، والأوسط للأصغر كذلك؛ وقد مرَّ من قبل ما يدل عليه أيضًا. وعلى هذا إذا كانت مطلقة عامة؛ إذ النتيجة وجودية لا دائمة لما مر . والرابع من المباحث هو أن تكون الكبرى عرفية خاصة، والعرفية الخاصة مع الصغرى الضرورية والدائمة لا تكون ملتئمة لما مر في المشروطة الخاصة. ثمَّ الصغرى إذا كانت من المشروطتين فالنتيجة مشروطة خاصة، وإذا كانت من العرفيتين فالنتيجة عرفية خاصة لما مر من قبل : هذا إذا كانت الصغرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب الموضوع. فأما إذا كانت من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب المحمول فالنتيجة وقتية خاصة. وكذلك إذا كانت من الوقتيتين خاصة كانت أو عامة ضرورية كانت أو لا ضرورية : فإن ما يلزم في كل صورة من هذه الصور فهو وقتية خاصة أيضًا لما مر . وأما إذا كانت الصغرى وجودية لا ضرورية أو وجودية لا دائمة أو مطلقة عامة فالنتيجة وجودية لا دائمة على ما عرف في الثالث. فإنه لا فرق بين أن تكون الكبرى مشروطة خاصة أو عرفية خاصة في الأحكام المخصوصة بالجهات فيما نحن بصدده. والبيان الذي مرَّ ذكره في أثناء القسم الأول من المختلطات يكفي كل قسم بل في كل صورة من كل قسم يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى. [ت] وأما الثالث من الأقسام فذلك من المختلطات ما يكون من الممكنتين مع الكل فالصغرى منهما خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى من الكل أي قضية ١٦٧) كانت من القضايا المذكورة. وفي هذا القسم من المباحث / أيضًا: الأول في
So it cannot be permanent for the description of the minor or for its essence, but it must be necessary for it according to the description only. Thus it is said: necessarily, every J is B as long as J, and necessarily, every B is A but not permanently but as long as B, so necessarily every J is A not permanently but as long as J. And if the minor is common-conventional, the conclusion is particular-conventional; because the major is permanent for the description of the middle which is permanent for the description of the minor, or for its essence, but it is not permanent for the description of the middle, so it cannot be permanent for the description of the minor, or for its essence, but it must be permanent for it according to the description. And if the minor is particular-conditional, the conclusion is particular-conditional, because the major holds for the middle in a certain manner in which the middle holds for the minor. And if it is particular-conventional, the conclusion is particular-conventional, as previously mentioned, because the major is permanent for the description of the middle which is permanent for the description of the minor, but it is not permanent for the description of the middle, so it cannot be permanent for the description of the minor, but it must be for it according to the description. This is when the minor is from the conditional-type and conventional-type from the subject side. As for when it is from the conditional-type and conventional-type from the predicate side, the conclusion is contrary to them, so that if it is general-conditional, the conclusion is particular-temporary, i.e., not admitting necessity and permanence. Thus it is said: necessarily every J is B as long as B, and necessarily every B is A not permanently but as long as B, so necessarily every J is A not permanently but as long as B. And similarly if the minor is general-conventional or particular-conventional from the conditional-type and conventional-type as previously. And similarly if it is from the temporary-type, whether particular or general, necessary or not necessary, then what follows in each form of these forms is particular-temporary; and the clarification in all is apparent. And if the minor is existential not necessary, then the conclusion is existential not permanent, because the major is not permanent for the middle. Even if it is necessary for it according to the description, it is not permanent for the minor, whether the middle is permanent for the minor or not. And similarly if it is existential not permanent, then it follows the minor in that case by necessity that the major is not permanent for the middle, and the middle is likewise for the minor; and what indicates that has also preceded. And upon this, if it is absolute general; for the conclusion is existential not permanent as previously. And the fourth of the investigations is that the major is particular-conventional, and the particular-conventional with the necessary and permanent minor do not combine as previously in the particular-conditional. Then if the minor is from the conditional-type, the conclusion is particular-conditional; if from the conventional-type, the conclusion is particular-conventional, as previously. This is when the minor is from the conditional-type and conventional-type from the subject side. As for when they are from the conditional-type and conventional-type from the predicate side, the conclusion is particular-temporary. And similarly if they are from the temporary-type, whether particular or general, necessary or not necessary, then what follows in each form of these forms is particular-temporary also, as previously. And if the minor is existential not necessary or existential not permanent or absolute general, the conclusion is existential not permanent according to what was known in the third. For there is no difference between the major being particular-conditional or particular-conventional in the rulings specific to modalities in what we are discussing. The explanation that preceded during the first section of mixed syllogisms suffices for every section, rather in every form of every section it is known by reflection, God willing. [T] And as for the third of the divisions, that is from the mixed syllogisms what is from the two possible premises with the rest, so the minor from them, whether particular or general, and the major from the rest, i.e., any proposition from the mentioned propositions. And in this section of the investigations also: first in
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a systematic discussion of how the modality (jiha) of the conclusion in a mixed syllogism is determined by the modalities of the premises, specifically focusing on cases where the major premise is particular-conventional and the minor premise has various modalities. The author begins by stating a principle: the major term cannot be permanently predicated of the minor simply by virtue of the minor's essence or description; rather, it must be necessary for it only under the description. He then illustrates with an example using variables J, B, A, showing that if the minor premise is a universal conditional (e.g., 'every J is B as long as J'), and the major is a non-permanent conditional (e.g., 'every B is A, not always, but as long as B'), the conclusion is also conditional (e.g., 'every J is A, not always, but as long as J'). He then proceeds to enumerate cases: if the minor is common-conventional (urfiyya aamma), the conclusion is particular-conventional (urfiyya khaassa), because the major is permanent for the description of the middle, but the middle is not permanent for the minor in a way that yields permanent predication. If the minor is particular-conditional (mashruta khaassa), the conclusion is also particular-conditional. If the minor is particular-conventional, the conclusion is also particular-conventional. These rules apply when the minor is from the subject-side conditional or conventional types. When the minor is from the predicate-side conditional or conventional types, the conclusion becomes particular-temporary (waqtiyya khaassa). For example, if the major is general-conditional and the minor is from the predicate side, the conclusion expresses a temporary connection. Similarly, if both premises are temporary (waqtiyya), the conclusion is also particular-temporary. When the minor is existential (wujoodiyya) without necessity, the conclusion is existential and non-permanent, because the major is not permanent for the middle. The author notes that the same reasoning applies when the minor is absolute general (mutlaqa aamma). He then introduces the fourth investigation: when the major is particular-conventional, it does not combine with a necessary or permanent minor, similar to the case with particular-conditional. Finally, he summarizes that when the major is particular-conditional or particular-conventional, the rules are the same for the modalities of the conclusion. The passage ends by introducing the third section of mixed syllogisms, which involves possible (mumkina) premises combined with the rest.

عرفية خاصة
وقتية خاصة
وجودية لا ضرورية
مطلقة عامة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the minor is 'absolute general' (mutlaqa aamma), the conclusion is:
A) General absolute (mutlaqa aamma)
B) Particular-conditional (mashruta khaassa)
C) Particular-temporary (waqtiyya khaassa)
D) Existential not permanent (wujoodiyya la da'ima)

Key Takeaway
An existential or absolute minor yields an existential non-permanent conclusion; a particular-conventional major cannot combine with necessary or permanent minors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a mixed syllogism, the minor is 'Some J is B' (wujoodiyya la daruriyya) and the major is 'Every B is A permanently' (dā'ima). According to the passage, what is the conclusion's modality? (Answer: wujoodiyya la da'ima)
?
If the major is particular-conventional (urfiyya khaassa) and the minor is necessary (darūriyya), does the syllogism combine according to the passage? (Answer: No, it does not combine, similar to the case with particular-conditional)

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Conclusions with Specific 'Urfiyya Major Premises
Author explains conclusions when major premise is 'urfiyya khaasa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal conclusion of a mixed syllogism when the major premise is 'urfiyya khassa, given the modal type and side of the minor premise.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality mirrors the minor's side and type, not the major's.

In modal syllogistics, not all premise combinations yield a valid conclusion. This passage specifies the rules when the major premise is a specific habitual ('urfiyya khassa). The minor premise's modality and whether it is on the subject or predicate side determine the conclusion's modality. Understanding these rules is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where modal reasoning appears.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise's modality
The passage assumes the major premise is 'urfiyya khassa (specific habitual). Recognize that this is a modal type indicating a customarily true but not necessary proposition.
2
Check compatibility with necessary/permanent minor
The text states that a specific 'urfiyya major does not harmonize with a necessary or permanent minor (similar to the specific conditioned case). This means no valid conclusion arises; the syllogism is invalid.
3
Determine the minor's modal type and side
The minor can be from various modal types: conditioned (mashrutatan), 'urfiyya, temporal (waqtia), or existence (wujudiyya). Also note whether it is on the subject side or predicate side of the conclusion? The passage uses 'from the side of the subject/predicate' meaning the subject or predicate of the conclusion? Actually, it refers to the minor premise's own subject/predicate orientation? Clarify: In the text, 'from the side of the subject' means the minor is a subject-term-focused modality? This is technical. For the guided path, we can simplify: check whether the minor's modality is on the subject or predicate side of the conclusion? Actually, the passage says: 'if the minor is from the two conditioned... from the subject side... if from the predicate side...' This likely refers to whether the modal condition in the minor attaches to the subject or predicate. We'll just note to pay attention to that distinction.
The 'side' refers to whether the conditional or 'urfiyya qualification applies to the subject term or predicate term of the proposition.
4
Apply the rule for each case
If the minor is from conditioned or 'urfiyya on the subject side, the conclusion has the same modality (conditioned or 'urfiyya). If on the predicate side, the conclusion is specific temporal (waqtia khassa). If the minor is from two temporals (any variety), conclusion is also specific temporal. If the minor is existence non-permanent or absolute general, conclusion is existence non-permanent.
These rules come from earlier discussions referenced in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من المباحث هو أن تكون الكبرى عرفية خاصة، والعرفية الخاصة مع الصغرى الضرورية والدائمة لا تكون ملتئمة لما مر في المشروطة الخاصة. ثمَّ الصغرى إذا كانت من المشروطتين فالنتيجة مشروطة خاصة، وإذا كانت من العرفيتين فالنتيجة عرفية خاصة لما مر من قبل : هذا إذا كانت الصغرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب الموضوع. فأما إذا كانت من المشروطتين والعرفيتين من جانب المحمول فالنتيجة وقتية خاصة. وكذلك إذا كانت من الوقتيتين خاصة كانت أو عامة ضرورية كانت أو لا ضرورية : فإن ما يلزم في كل صورة من هذه الصور فهو وقتية خاصة أيضًا لما مر . وأما إذا كانت الصغرى وجودية لا ضرورية أو وجودية لا دائمة أو مطلقة عامة فالنتيجة وجودية لا دائمة على ما عرف في الثالث. فإنه لا فرق بين أن تكون الكبرى مشروطة خاصة أو عرفية خاصة في الأحكام المخصوصة بالجهات فيما نحن بصدده. والبيان الذي مرَّ ذكره في أثناء القسم الأول من المختلطات يكفي كل قسم بل في كل صورة من كل قسم يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
The fourth investigation is that the major premise is a specific 'urfiyya, and the specific 'urfiyya with the necessary and permanent minor does not harmonize as mentioned in the specific conditioned [premises]. Then, if the minor is from the two conditioned [premises], the conclusion is a specific conditioned [premise]; if from the two 'urfiyya, the conclusion is a specific 'urfiyya as previously mentioned: this is when the minor is from the two conditioned and 'urfiyya on the subject side. But if it is from the two conditioned and 'urfiyya on the predicate side, the conclusion is a specific temporal. Similarly, if from the two temporal, whether specific or general, necessary or not necessary: for what follows in each form of these forms is also a specific temporal as previously mentioned. And if the minor is an existence non-necessary or existence non-permanent or absolute general, the conclusion is an existence non-permanent as known in the third. For there is no difference between the major being a specific conditioned or a specific 'urfiyya in the rulings specific to modalities in what we are dealing with. And the demonstration mentioned in the first part of the mixed syllogisms suffices for every part, rather in every form of every part it is known by reflection, if God wills.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed syllogisms (al-mukhtalitat). The author addresses the fourth investigation (al-rabi' min al-mabahith) concerning cases where the major premise is a specific 'urfiyya (عرفية خاصة). 'Urfiyya is a modal category meaning 'habitual' or 'customary' — something that holds in the usual course but not necessarily. The specific 'urfiyya is contrasted with the specific conditioned (al-mashrutat al-khassa). The author states that when the major is specific 'urfiyya, it does not combine well with a necessary or permanent minor, similar to the case of specific conditioned premises (as previously discussed). Then, varying with the nature of the minor, the conclusion changes: if the minor is from the conditioned or 'urfiyya on the subject side, the conclusion mirrors the minor's modality; if on the predicate side, the conclusion is a specific temporal (waqtia khassa). If the minor is existence (wujudiyya) non-necessary or non-permanent or absolute general, the conclusion is existence non-permanent. The author asserts that the same demonstration given in the first part of mixed syllogisms applies to all these cases.

Key Terms
عرفية خاصة ('urfiyya khassa): A proposition that is habitually true but not necessarily; specific habitual as opposed to general habitual. [Note: This term is specific to modal logic; not common in later logic texts.]
مشروطة خاصة (mashrutat khassa): A conditional proposition with a specific condition; used in modal syllogistic. [Note: Similar in later texts but may have variations.]
وقتية خاصة (waqtia khassa): A temporal proposition with a specific time. [Note: Stable across tradition.]
وجودية لا دائمة (wujudiyya la da'ima): A proposition that affirms existence but not permanently; contingent existence. [Note: Standard modal term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Specific conditioned
B) Specific 'urfiyya
C) Specific temporal
D) Existence non-permanent

Key Takeaway
The conclusion's modality in mixed syllogisms with 'urfiyya khassa major is determined by the minor's modality and side, not the major's.
